Title: Tensor invariants for multipartite entanglement classification

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2604.02269v1

Published Time: Fri, 03 Apr 2026 01:19:11 GMT

Number of Pages: 110

Markdown Content:
# Tensor invariants for multipartite entanglement classification 

## Sylvain Carrozza ∗1, Johann Chevrier †1,2 , and Luca Lionni ‡21Universit´ e Bourgogne Europe, CNRS, IMB UMR 5584, 21000 Dijon, France 

> 2

ENS de Lyon, CNRS, LPENSL, UMR 5672, 69342 Lyon cedex 07, France 

## April 3, 2026 

Abstract 

Organising the space of entanglement structures of a multipartite quantum system is a much more challenging task than its bipartite version: while the local unitary ( LU ) orbit of a bipartite pure state can be conveniently characterized by its entanglement spectrum, invariants of multipartite entanglement structures are comparatively difficult to define and work with. The root cause of this difference is that the bipartite problem can be reduced to the analysis of matrix invariants, while its multipartite version is governed by a much richer space of tensor invariants. The present work explores the latter through the lens of so-called trace-invariants, which are in one-to-one correspondence with combinatorial objects known as colored graphs. We first explain why trace-invariant evaluations can serve as labels of LU -orbits of multipartite pure states, how this strategy extends to random states, and how the effect of local operations ( LO ) can be analyzed through such data. We then focus on entanglement classification within an (infinite-dimensional) subspace of reference states, whose basic building blocks are GHZ states of various dimensions. We show that relatively simple subclasses of trace-invariants are sufficient to separate the LU -orbits of reference states, and enable a complete (resp. an incomplete) characterization of their relations in the LO (resp. LOCC ) resource theory of entanglement. Finally, we investigate how a (still infinite) subclass of reference states of local dimension N can be efficiently distinguished at leading and subleading orders in an asymptotic large-N expansion (among themselves, or from Haar-random states). This analysis relies crucially on combinatorial quantities associated to colored graphs, some of which have already played instrumental roles in the recent literature on random tensors. Results of broader relevance are reported along the way.

# 1 Introduction and summary of our main results 

If, historically, the notion of entanglement was first articulated in thought-experiments to address potential limita-tions of the quantum formalism [1], almost a century later, it is recognized as an unavoidable ingredient of quantum theory [2–4], that is also of great practical relevance: indeed, it is arguably the main resource powering the on-going development of quantum technologies. On the mathematical side, a large body of works has been dedicated to the precise definition and characterization of entanglement, both in the bipartite and multipartite settings (see e.g. [5, 6] and references therein). However, it is fair to say that the latter is comparatively much less understood than the former, for reasons that can all be traced back to the following observation: while a bipartite pure state can be naturally represented by a matrix ,whose spectrum (understood as its set of singular values) characterizes its entanglement structure, a multipartite pure state is naturally encoded into a higher order tensor , whose space of invariants turns out to be dramatically more complicated than that of a matrix. 1 In particular, no simple and universal notion of spectrum is available to characterize the algebra of invariants of a tensor. As a result, one should expect the development of a complete theory of multipartite entanglement to be intrinsically more challenging than that of bipartite entanglement theory, and to heavily rely on tensors rather than matrices. The present manuscript makes a step in that direction by taking advantage of a combinatorial toolbox that was primarily developed to represent and manipulate tensor invariants in the theory of random tensors (see e.g. [7, 8] and references therein). As this work will illustrate, this toolbox is perfectly suited to the definition and analysis of tensor invariants in the theory of multipartite entanglement. The fundamental classification problem of entanglement theory can be formulated as follows. Given a pure state 

|ψy P H1 b¨ ¨ ¨b HD in a global Hilbert space that has been partitioned into D subsystems (represented by the tensor factors tHcu), the goal is to determine which states in H1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b HD merely differ from |ψy by the combined action of unitary operators that are local to the subsystems tHcu (or equivalently, that differ by a change of orthonormal basis in each tensor factor Hc). Any such operation is called a Local Unitary (LU ) transformation, and any two     

> 1For the same reason, characterizing the entanglement structure of a bipartite mixed state, which can be represented by a tripartite
> pure state, is challenging. In this respect, we can consider the theory of bipartite entanglement for mixed states as a branch of the theory of multipartite entanglement.

2states related by a LU -transformation are deemed to have equivalent LU -entanglement structures (relative to the 

D-partitioning H1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b HD of the global Hilbert space). Ideally, one would like to be able to distingish any two inequivalent LU -entanglement structures or, in mathematical terms, to separate any two orbits of the local unitary group. Furthermore, in the context of quantum information processing , one is interested in understanding how distinct entanglement structures may or may not be transformed into one another given a set of quantum channels that are operationally available. This type of question gives rise to operational resource theories [9, 10] of entanglement such as the widely used framework of Local Operations and Classical Communication ( LOCC )[11–15]. The latter formalizes the experimental situation in which D agents are allowed to perform any quantum operation they like in their respective quantum laboratories ( LO ), and can also communicate with each other by classical means e.g. they can phone each other ( CC ). From a mathematical point of view, such a resource theory defines a preorder on the set of D-partite states which, ideally, one would like to completely characterize. Given that this preorder is compatible with the underlying LU structure, a natural strategy is to construct a sufficiently rich family of real LU -invariants which vary monotonically under any quantum transformation allowed by the given resource theory ( e.g. a LOCC transformation). Before listing the specific questions we would like to address in the multipartite setting, let us briefly recall a number of relevant results in the bipartite setting ( i.e. D “ 2). For a bipartite pure state |ψy P H1 b H2, by far the most studied quantitative measure of entanglement is its entanglement entropy , which is nothing but the von Neumann entropy of its reduced density matrix on either subsystem: that is, letting e.g. ρ1 def

[16, 18–20]). However, Sp| ψyq being non-polynomial in |ψy (or in the eigenvalues of ρ1), evaluating it explicitly can be challenging. What’s more, the notion of entanglement spectrum is also convenient to characterize order relations in resource theories of entanglement: for instance, the LOCC preorder on bipartite pure states has been fully characterized in terms of majorization conditions on entanglement spectra [12]. In addition to their practical use in computations, entanglement R´ enyi entropies have direct operational rel-evance in certain contexts. They are for instance useful in deriving entanglement inequalities, as e.g. the second entanglement R´ enyi entropy provides strong entanglement criteria that can outperform standard Bell inequalities (see Refs. [24–26]). They are also instrumental in the resource theory of catalytic LOCC [27, 28], where majoriza-tion conditions which can be directly expressed in terms of entanglement R´ enyi entropies relax and replace the majorization conditions of Ref. [12]; hence, in that particular context, the values of entanglement R´ enyi entropies are more directly operationally relevant than any individual eigenvalue of the reduced density matrix. Finally, if the bipartite state |ψy is sampled from a probability distribution, one is often interested in determining its typical entanglement structure in a regime of large dimension, where measure concentration phenomena are expected to be at play. For definiteness, let the Hilbert spaces H1 and H2 have the same dimension N , and let |ψy

3be drawn uniformly at random in the set of pure states on H1 b H2. Then, the following asymptotic expression holds: A

[29]), which states that xSp| ψyqy “ ln pN q´ 1{2`op1q. Given that ln pN q is the entanglement entropy (resp. entanglement R´ enyi-k entropy) of a (maximally entangled) Bell state of dimension N , those relations tell us that the uniform random state is undistinguishable from that deterministic state at leading order in N , but not so at the next-to-leading order. Relations such as Eq. (3) have been generalized beyond the uniform distribution in the context of random tensor networks [30], where it was shown that the typical entanglement entropy of a bipartition is governed by an equation whose holographic features are reminiscent of the Ryu-Takayanagi formula. Let us come back to the multipartite setting ( D ě 3). Our key entry-point into this world is that, even though no simple generalization of the notion of entanglement spectrum (or, even, of entanglement entropy) is currently available in that regime, the polynomials tTr ρk 

> 1

ukPN˚ appearing in entanglement R´ enyi entropies (with integer k)

do admit natural multipartite generalizations: namely, through the notion of trace-invariant previously introduced in the theory of random tensors (see Ref. [7] and references therein). Furthermore, trace-invaritants of a D-partite system are in one-to-one correspondence with D-colored graphs , and are thus intrinsically combinatorial objects. Our aim in the present article (and in follow-up works), is to systematically develop a quantitative theory of multipartite entanglement based on trace-invariants and their underlying combinatorial structure. In particular, we will address the following questions: (i) How can one efficiently distinguish LU -equivalence classes by means of trace-invariants in a multipartite system? (ii) How should one combine trace-invariants to obtain valuable monotones in resource theories of entanglement such as LO , LOCC or LOSR ? Can the underlying preorder be efficiently characterized by means of trace-invariants? (iii) How can the typical entanglement structure of a large random state be characterized in terms of correlation functions of trace-invariants? Furthermore, in connection to the third question, we will investigate the implications of a recently discovered property of random tensors, that is somewhat surprising and subtle. As it turns out, a hallmark of random matrix theory that is often expected (and sometimes assumed) for random tensor distributions – the large-N factorization property of correlation functions – is not generally valid for random tensors [31, 32]. We will see that this result has direct implications for question (iii). Besides, while investigating them, we were naturally led to address a question that we expect to be of independent interest to random tensor enthusiasts: (iv) Can one generate richer infinite families of trace-invariants than the ones currently available, whose Gaussian (or Haar) moments can be proven to factorize over their connected components at leading order in the large-N

limit? It goes without saying that the present work is not the first to explore questions (i)-(iii). For instance, to mention just a selection of relevant contributions to the literature on this subject: the role of trace-invariants for entanglement classification was recognized in e.g. Refs. [33, 34] (under a different name), multipartite entanglement properties of random tensor networks have been explored by means of observables which are indeed trace-invariants [35–38], and recent efforts have been initiated to construct valuable entanglement monotones from trace-invariants [39–41]. More broadly, multipartite aspects of entanglement theory seem to be catching the attention of a growing number of researchers, from various corners of physics and mathematics. Our aim with the present manuscript is to initiate a systematic assessment of the role of trace-invariants in multipartite entanglement classification problems, while taking advantage of a combinatorial toolbox that remains largely untapped in that context, and that we anticipate to be of great practical value to streamline and generalize constructions that have already appeared in the literature. We also made an effort to approach this problem in a largely self-contained manner, that will hopefully speak to a diverse community of researchers. 2

Let us conclude this introduction by an informal summary of our main results, which may also serve as a reader’s guide. 

> 2This explains in part the significant page count of the present contribution.

4In Sec. 2 , we start out by recalling some basic definitions and properties of local unitary symmetry, and reviewing the main combinatorial properties of trace-invariants that are relevant for the rest of the paper. In Prop. 2.8, we recall in particular that trace-invariants do separate LU -orbits, a result which in principle provides a complete answer to question (i). However, in a multipartite quantum state space of even moderately large dimension, the number of independent trace-invariants is expected to be impractically large: on the one hand, the best known bounds on the number of invariants in a generating set grow extremely rapidly with the dimension (as we recall in Prop. 2.11, which quotes a result of Ref. [34]); on the other hand, the number of connected trace-invariants of a given order k grows super-exponentially with k in the multipartite setting [42], which starkly contrasts with the bipartite setting (where there exists a single connected invariant of a given order). In Sec. 2.4, we introduce definitions and concepts that allow to precisely formalize question (iii): we first extend the definition of LU -equivalence to random states (Def. 2.12), and then to sequences of (random or deterministic) states indexed by a dimensional parameter N P N˚ (Def. 2.14). Def. 2.14 also introduces two notions of large-N asymptotic 

LU -equivalence (one weaker than the other), that provide coarser notions of entanglement structure classification than the exact Def. 2.12, and that are relevant for generalized Page curve calculations. In Sec. 2.5, we return to the classification problem of deterministic multipartite pure states, demonstrating in Prop. 2.18 that a subset of trace-invariants – which we refer to as genuinely D-partite – are sufficient to characterize separable D-partite states. Sec. 2.6 explores the notion of coarse-graining of a D-partite state space ( i.e. of turning it into a D1-partite state space with D1 ă D) in connection to trace-invariants, their graph-theoretical representations, and coarse-graining maps that can be directly implemented at the graphical level. In particular, Prop. 2.22 characterizes states that are genuinely entangled on a given D-partite state space, while Prop. 2.23 characterizes properties related to the notion of partial separability directly in terms of trace-invariants. In Sec. 2.7, we investigate question (ii) in the simple context of LO transformations, which form an integral part of any resource theory of entanglement (such as LOCC ). Prop. 2.28 provides a characterization of the LO preorder in terms trace-invariants, which specializes to a well-known result in the bipartite setting. While this characterization is not constructive and therefore not necessarily easy to apply in practice, its main corollary (Cor. 2.33) is: it provides an infinite family of inequalities that are necessarily obeyed by any LO transformation. This corollary allows introducing multipartite generalizations of entanglement R´ enyi-k entropies for integer k that are labeled by a D-colored graph rather than an integer (see Ex. 2.35): in particular, such quantities are LO -monotones. A key difference with the bipartite setting at this level is that trace-invariants are not in general positive (or even real), which results into the fact that higher order entanglement R´ enyi entropies may take arbitrarily large values, or be strictly infinite (see Ex. 2.38). The dramatic proliferation of the number of independent invariants in the multipartite entanglement classifica-tion problem (a manifestation of the fact that this is a tensor rather than a matrix classification problem) makes it unreasonable to look for complete characterizations of LU -classes when the dimension of the state space is even moderately large. On the other hand, such a feat is probably not required for practical applications where, resources being necessarily finite, it may be satisfactory enough to be able to classify a limited template of LU -equivalence classes. In turn, inequivalent entanglement stuctures drawn from such a limited template might be perfectly dis-tinguishable with the help of a limited number of trace-invariants. In this spirit, Sec. 3 introduces infinite families of invariants, and infinite families of states, that are structured enough to evade the fundamental challenge of the proliferation of multipartite LU -classes. The infinite families of trace-invariants introduced in Sec. 3.1 are in part im-ported from the existing literature, and in part new. In both situations, we provide graph-theoretic representations (as D-colored graphs) of such invariants that prove highly valuable to build-up intuition and reason about them. We encourage the reader to skip this section on a first reading, and only refer to it as needed when going through the rest of the paper. Sec. 3.2 introduces the limited template of states investigated in later sections. We first define an infinite family of deterministic states that we refer to as reference states : their basic buidling blocks are GHZ states of arbitrary dimension shared by an arbitrary number of subsystems. We provide a visual representation of such states in terms of hyper-graphs, and discuss the effect of coarse-graining in this combinatorial encoding. Second, we recall how the Haar-random state on a D-partite state space of local dimension N can be defined, how it can be analyzed with the help of Weingarten calculus, and how its properties compare to those of a Gaussian random tensor (see Sec. 3.2.2 and App. A). This is the only random state we will explicitly consider in the present work, even though some of the results from Sec. 6 will apply to more general distributions. In Sec. 3.3, we provide a complete classification of the LU -orbits of reference states with a help of a limited number of trace-invariants: Thm. 3.3 shows that D-partite reference states generate a p2D ´ D ´ 1q-parameter family of LU -orbits, which can be perfectly distinguished by the joint data of multi-entropies and reflected multi-entropies (whose respective defi-nitions are recalled in Sec. 3.1). In Sec. 3.4, we illustrate the two asymptotic notions of LU -equivalence introduced in Sec. 3.4. In particular, we invoke the failure of large-N factorization for Gaussian random tensors to prove that, unlike in D “ 2, in D ě 3 it is rigorously impossible to reproduce all the features of the leading-order entanglement 5structure of a Haar-random state with a deterministic state (see Prop. 3.5). In Sec. 4 , we restrict our attention further to an infinite subclass of reference states: those that can be defined on a D-partite Hilbert space with uniform local dimension N , and obey an additional condition on the allowed dimension of any GHZ building block. This set-up allows formulating and investigating an asymptotic version of question (i): how to efficiently distinguish LU -equivalence classes of reference states at leading order in the large-N

limit ? In other words, our goal in that section is to find a way to distinguish LU -classes by the sole knowledge of the leading-order exponents of trace-invariant evaluations in an asympotic 1 {N expansion. In Sec. 4.1, we first show that such scaling parameters can be conveniently expressed in terms of combinatorial quantities attached to the representation of trace-invariants by D-colored graphs, which have already played a major role in the theory of random tensors, such as: the number of faces of a D-colored graph, its genus when D “ 3, and its so-called 

Gurau degree for arbitrary D ě 3. In addition, we introduce the new notion of p-complete degree that naturally generalizes the Gurau degree (see App. B). A comparison of the scaling exponents associated to such reference states and those associated to the Haar-random state singles out a combinatorial quantity known as the degree of compatibility , which was recently introduced in Ref. [43]. Since the degree of compatibility always vanishes in the bipartite case and does not contribute to the leading-order exponents of reference states, its study is particularly relevant to question (iii) (and the related question (iv)): it is the combinatorial quantity that allows distinguishing the typical multipartite entanglement structure of the uniform random state from that of any reference state. However, the numerical search for invariants capable of distinguishing the subfamily of reference states associated with combinatorial quantities quickly becomes intractable, both due to the rapid growth of the quantities involved and the computational difficulty of efficiently evaluating certain quantities, particularly the degree of compatibility (which is defined implicitely via a min, see Eq. (230)). This motivates a deeper investigation of these combinatorial quantities, as developed in Sec. 4.2. Such an analysis highlights a number of characteristic properties (melonic, planar, compatible, etc.) associated with the invariants typically considered in the literature. As a result, in Sec 4.3 we classify the invariants introduced in Sec. 3.1 based on the values of the combinatorial quantities. In particular, we prove the compatibility or incompatibility of certain invariants (see also App. C) and, building on numerical computations, conjecture the exact value of the degree of compatibility for specific families, a proof of which remains elusive to date. Lastly, we compute the entropies associated with the families of trace invariants introduced in Sec. 3.1 for both reference and random states. This provides a preliminary approach to analyzing and understanding the meaning of these proposed multipartite entanglement entropies. Notably, multi-entropies [38, 39] and reflected multi-entropies [44, 45] stand out as particularly insightful quantities: despite some limitations, they remain among the few entropies capable of distinguishing several reference states, thus highlighting their potential for characterizing and comparing entanglement patterns in multipartite systems. 

Sec. 5 is where we return to question (ii), now specialized to reference states. In Sec. 5.1, we first invoke necessary conditions previously derived in Sec. 2.7 to establish or exclude a number of LO relations among reference states. In particular, we use reference states to prove that the sufficient conditions on LO relations established in Cor. 2.33 are not necessary. This motivates a new approach in Sec. 5.2, which relies on the exact (but somewhat implicit) characterization of the LO preorder from Prop. 2.28. Applying the conditions provided by this result to certain infinite families of D-colored graphs (that have been specifically introduced in Sec. 3 for that purpose), we arrive at Thm. 5.3. The proof of this theorem showcases the value of the combinatorial approach to trace-invariants we are following, which allows us to efficiently design and manipulate polynomial invariants of large degree. It results in a complete characterization of the LO preorder on reference states, that is both explicit and rather simple to state: the LU -orbit of a reference state |ψαy can be labeled by an integer-valued function α, and given a second reference state |ψβ y, we have |ψαy ÝÑ  

> LO

|ψβ y if and only if α divides β. In Sec. 5.3, we turn to a similar analysis of the LOCC preorder on reference states. Even though we were not able to fully characterize this preorder at this stage, Props. 5.7, 5.11 and 5.13 establish a number of sufficient conditions for two reference states to be related by LOCC (and, needless to say, all three results go beyond the LO relations established in Sec. 5.2). Those partial results rely on a relatively simple subset of LOCC protocols, all based on measurements followed by at most one round of one-way communication; this includes in particular a quantum teleportation protocol in Prop. 5.13. We leave the question of a more complete characterization of the LOCC preorder on reference states open for future work. In Sec. 6 , we explore combinatorial strategies allowing to design trace-invariants with specified entanglement distinction properties (question (i)). We focus again on the question of how best to distinguish reference states among themselves, or to distinguish a Haar-distributed random state from a deterministic reference state. To this effect, we start out by analysing the behavior of the combinatorial quantities used in the LU -orbit classification of Sec. 4, 6under the action of three types of binary operations: unions, flips, and vertex contractions of pairs of D-colored graphs (see Def. 6.1). Of particular interest are the results of Thm. 6.4 which, under favorable conditions, provide a recursive approach to the computation of the degree of compatibility of a graph. Without going into too much detail, one of the favorable conditions entailing this result is that the graph under consideration only admits tree-like 

contributions to the leading order in the Haar-random state (see Def. 6.3, App. D, as well as our companion paper [46]). Another case covered by Thm. 6.4 is when the graph under consideration is the result of a binary operation on two graphs of sufficiently small degree of compatibility. Altogether, Thm. 6.4 can be applied to a significant fraction of the invariants introduced in Sec. 3.1. The particularly interesting case of so-called maximally single-trace 

invariants [47] is discussed in and around Prop. 6.5: while they all appear to be compatible for small enough values of D and small enough number of vertices, recent results on the large-N factorization problem in random tensor theory [32] suggest that incompatible maximally single-trace graphs should exist. Indeed, an example of such graph is provided in Fig. 44. Some applications of binary operations and Thm. 6.4 are finally explored in Sec. 6.3. To begin with, Sec. 6.3.1 is devoted to the important question of the large-N asymptotic evaluation of generalized R´ enyi entropies in the Haar-distributed random state of local dimension N . In contrast to the bipartite setting, taking the average of a trace-invariant and its logarithm are operations that can fail to approximately commute in the large-N regime, precisely due to the non-factorization phenomenon uncovered in Ref. [31]. Moreover, the non-positive character of certain multipartite trace-invariants implies that we cannot always bound a generalized R´ enyi entropy by a constant times ln pN q, which also creates difficulties. Nonetheless, Props. 6.11, which is proven in full in our companion article (Ref. [46]), establishes rigorous conditions on a D-colored graph and a random state that allow to determine the typical value of the associated generalized R´ enyi entropy in that state. Similarly, with an additional assumption on the invariant, Prop. 6.18 (also proven in Ref. [46]) allows computing the leading and subleading contributions to the expectation value of the generalized R´ enyi entropy at large N , in very much the same way as Eq. (3) can be derived in a bipartite setting. We apply these results to specific families of D-colored graphs in various corollaries. Finally, we conclude by providing concrete illustrations of tree-based designs of trace-invariants in Secs. 6.3.2, 6.3.3 and 6.3.4. Question (iv) and its implications are more thoroughly explored in a companion publication, Ref. [46]. Moreover, in a third upcoming publication, we will return to question (ii) by introducing and studying the properties of richer families of entanglement monotones than the ones we restricted our attention to in the present work.

# 2 Local unitary symmetry and its invariants 

## 2.1 Local unitary groups and local unitary equivalence 

A D-partite quantum state space is a Hilbert space H together with a partition into D local subsystems, represented by Hilbert spaces H1, . . . , HD , so that H “ H1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b HD . We will assume each local subsystem to be finite-dimensional, and denote by Nc def 

“ dim pHcq the dimension of the c-th subsystem. For notational convenience, we label such a D-partite state space by the D-tuple F “ p H1, . . . , HD q of its local tensor factors and set HF def 

“ H1 b¨ ¨ ¨b HD .In the present paper, we focus exclusively on the entanglement structure of D-partite pure states, which can be represented by unit vectors in HF. We denote by SpHFq the unit sphere in HF. Recall that a pure state |ψy P SpHFq

is said to be separable if it can be written in the form |ψy “ | v1yb¨ ¨ ¨b| vD y with |vcy P SpHcq for any c P t 1 , . . . , D u;when this is not possible, |ψy is said to be entangled .The group of local unitary transformations of HF is the group 

LU F def 

“ UpH1q ˆ ¨ ¨ ¨ ˆ UpHD q , (4) where, for any Hilbert space H, UpHq denotes its unitary group. LU F acts in an obvious way on SpHFq and partitions it into orbits, the set of which we denote by 

OF def 

“ ␣LU F |ψy | | ψy P SpHFq( . (5) The LU -entanglement structure of a pure state |ψy P HF is defined to be its orbit LU F |ψy. In more physical (but somewhat imprecise) terms, the LU -entanglement structure of |ψy captures any property of that state that is 7invariant under changes of orthonormal bases in the local subsystems H1 , . . . , HD . Accordingly, we will say that two states |ψy , |ϕy P SpHFq are LU F-equivalent – noted |ψy „

> LU F

|ϕy – if they lie in the same LU F orbit; in other words: 

|ψy „ 

> LU F

|ϕy ô LU F |ψy “ LU F |ϕy ô Dp U1, . . . , U D q P UpH1q ˆ . . . ˆ UpHD q , |ψy “ p U1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b UD q | ϕy . (6) As an example, any two separable states in HF are LU F-equivalent since, for any c P t 1, . . . , D u, UpHcq acts transitively on SpHcq. By contrast, two entangled states are not necessarily in the same orbit: the partition of the quantum state space into local unitary orbits provides a much finer classification of entanglement than the simple dichotomy between separable and entangled states. The main purpose of the present article is to investigate the properties of particular families of polynomial invariants allowing to distinguish distinct LU -entanglement structures, or equivalently, to separate points in the set of orbits OF. Before turning to this question, let us introduce a slightly more general equivalence relation, which allows comparing D-partite states that do not live in the same D-partite state space. For this purpose, we will say that a map V : HF Ñ HF1

## 2.2 Definition and basic properties of trace-invariants 

Let us fix a D-partite state space F “ p H1, . . . , HD q, and choose a reference orthonormal basis t| iycu1ďiďNc in each 

Hc (1 ď c ď D). For any state |ψy P SpHFq, we denote by tψi1¨¨¨ iD u its complex coordinates relative to this choice of local bases, namely 

|ψy “

“ P p| ψyq .Trace-invariants are particular homogeneous polynomials from AF 

> inv

that can be labeled by D-colored bipartite graphs. As we will explain here and in the next subsections, they are particularly convenient to work with, thanks to the following facts: a) the simple way in which they are defined; b) they generate the full invariant algebra AF

> inv

;c) they are universal , in the sense that they can be defined on any D-partite state space. In fact, we will think of the collection of all trace-invariants as providing a generalization to D ě 3 of the notion of entanglement spectrum 9of a bipartite pure state (in D “ 2, trace-invariants can be expressed in terms of elementary power sums of elements from this spectrum). In terms of the tensor components defined in Eq. (20), trace-invariants [7] are the class of homogeneous LU -invariant polynomials obtained by contraction of k ě 1 copies of the tensor ψ “ t ψi1...i D u with k copies of the conjugate tensor ψ “ t ψi1...i D u, with the constraint that an index of ψ in position c P t 1, . . . , D u need to be summed over with an index of ψ in the same position. The label c is often referred to as the color of the index, and we will make use of this nomenclature in the rest of the paper. More concretely, we will make use of two complementary representations of trace-invariants, which we now recall. 

Trace-invariants and colored graphs. Trace-invariants can be encoded into certain edge-decorated multi-graphs, which are known in the random tensor literature as colored graphs (see Refs. [7, 8, 48]). 3 We represent the tensor ψ “ t ψi1...i D u and its complex conjugate ψ “ t ψi1...i D u as D-valent white and black vertices, respectively (see Fig. 1). Each half-edge carries a color corresponding to the position c P t 1, . . . , D u of the index ic. The 

Figure 1: Graphical representation of ψ and ψ.summation of the indices in position c (that is, of color c) between a ψ and a ψ is represented graphically as an edge of color c connecting the corresponding black and white vertices. With these graphical rules, a choice of invariant contraction pattern of all the indices of k copies of ψ and k copies of ψ can be represented by a decorated graph with 2 k vertices which is bipartite (any edge connects a black vertex to a white vertex) and D-edge-colored (each vertex has exactly one incident edge of color c for every color c P t 1, . . . , D u). We let GD (resp. Gc 

> D

) denote the set of bipartite D-edge-colored graphs (resp. connected bipartite D-edge-colored graphs), and use the notation Tr Gp¨q 

for the trace-invariant represented by a graph G P GD . Given G P GD , kpGq P N˚ will denote the number of white (resp. black) vertices of G (equivalently, kpGq is the degree in the coordinates tψi1¨¨¨ iD u of the homogeneous polynomial Tr G). In the following, we simply refer to such graphs as D-colored graphs (or simply as colored graphs ). For D “ 2 and F “ p H1, H2q, a colored graph is a collection of cycles whose edges are alternatively of colors 1 and 2. We let ρ1 “ Tr H2 p| ψy x ψ|q P End pH1q and ρ2 “ Tr H1 p| ψy x ψ|q P End pH2q. Given a choice of local bases as in Eq. (20), |ψy is represented by an N1 ˆ N2 complex matrix ψ “ p ψi1,i 2 q, and ρ1 (resp. ρ2) is therefore represented by the matrix ψψ : (resp. ψ:ψ). The trace-invariant associated with a colored graph Ck consisting of a single cycle with k white vertices (see the left of Fig. 2) is then Tr Ck p| ψyq “ Tr H1 pρk 

> 1

Tr Ck1 p| ψyq ¨ ¨ ¨ Tr Ckp p| ψyq , for some p ě 1 and k1 ě ¨ ¨ ¨ ě kp ą 0.

Our goal in the following will be to compare the entanglement structures of various D-partite states by evaluating trace-invariants on them. That is, we will consider two D-partite entanglement structures to be “close” whenever the evaluation of any trace-invariant on them gives “close” results. At the most basic level, this idea can be implemented by equipping the space of orbits OF of a D-partite state space F with a topology that makes all trace-invariants continuous (or, equivalently, that makes any f P AF 

> inv

continuous). The coarsest such topology is the Zariski topology, namely, the topology on OF whose closed sets are of the form V pIq def 

“ t O P OF | @ P P I , P pOq “ 0u,where I is a subset of AF

> inv

.5 Any quantitative approach to entanglement based on trace-invariants can only be consistent if OF is equipped with a topology that is finer than the Zariski topology. We will work under this very mild assumption in the rest of the paper, without committing to any particular such choice. In future works, it will be particularly interesting to investigate whether OF can be equipped with an operationally-meaningful metric structure that can be directly expressed in terms of trace-invariants (note the emphasis on “operationally-meaningful”). 

## 2.4 LU -equivalence of random states, and asymptotic LU -equivalence of sequences of states 

To be able to discuss properties of random entangled states, let us now generalize the notion of LU -equivalence slightly. We will say that |ψy is a random D-partite state if |ψy is a random variable on SpHFq for some D-partite state space F, such that the expectation value of any trace-invariant of |ψy is well-defined and finite: 

@G P GD , xTr Gp| ψyqy P C . (49) There is then a natural generalization of the notion of LU -equivalence that applies to such random states while remaining consistent with the previously described deterministic setting. 

Definition 2.12. Let |ψy and |ϕy be two D-partite random states. We say that |ψy is LU -equivalent to |ϕy – noted 

|ψy „  

> LU

|ϕy – if: 

@G P GD , xTr Gp| ψyqy “ x Tr Gp| ϕyqy . (50) 

Remark 2.13. Note that, crucially, G is not restricted to be connected in the previous definition, unlike in Prop. 2.8. However, for deterministic states ( i.e. random states whose distributions are Dirac delta functions), xTr Gp| ψyqy and 

xTr Gp| ϕyqy factorize over the connected components of G, and one recovers the characterization of LU -equivalence given in Prop. 2.8. 

In the present paper, we are particularly interested in distinguishing entangled states in the asymptotic limit of 

N Ñ `8 , where N is a dimension parameter. We formalize two weaker notions of LU -equivalence that are sufficient for such distinctions: asymptotic LU -equivalence on the one hand, and LU -equivalence in scaling on the other hand.

Remark 2.15. Given G P GD , it is convenient to denote by G the colored graph obtained from G by flipping the colors of all its vertices. One then has Tr Gp¨q “ Tr Gp¨q . According to the first item in the previous list of definitions, one trivially has that: pG, ψ q obeys the large-N Ansatz if and only if pG, ψ q does. Furthermore, in that case: sGpψq “ sGpψq and μGpψq “ μGpψq.

Remark 2.16. When ψ and ϕ are both deterministic sequences, the previous definitions can be simplified: expec-tation values can be omitted, and since trace-invariants factorize over their connected components, it suffices to compare connected invariants to assess equivalence. Furthermore, for ψ and ϕ deterministic, sG is clearly additive under decomposition into connected components, while μG is multiplicative, or in other words: for any G1, G 2 P GD ,

sG1\G2 “ sG1 ` sG2 and μG1\G2 “ μG1 .μ G2 . This is not always true for random states, see Refs. [31, 32] and Sec. 6.3.1. 

It is clear that LU -equivalence implies asymptotic LU -equivalence, which itself implies LU -equivalence in scaling; moreover, none of the reverse implications hold. 

## 2.5 Characterizing separable states by means of trace-invariants 

We have seen in Prop. 2.8 that the entanglement structure of a (deterministic) D-partite pure state |ψy P H1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b 

HD is fully characterized by the trace-invariant evaluations tTr Gp| ψyqu GPGc 

> D

. In practice, one is often interested in answering much coarsest classification questions, such as: is |ψy separable or entangled? As we will see shortly, such a question can be answered by evaluating only one suitably chosen trace-invariant. In the well-known bipartite context ( D “ 2), it suffices to measure e.g. the 2-purity of the reduced density matrix on one of the subsystems: 

|ψy is then separable if and only if the 2-purity is unity. 7 It turns out that such simple tests of separability can also be obtained when D ě 3. To describe them, we introduce the following subclass of trace-invariants. 

Definition 2.17. Let D ě 2, G P GD , and c1, c 2 P t 1, . . . , D u.We will say that the colors c1, c 2 are parallel in G if the graph obtained by removing all the edges of color c R t c1, c 2u

from G is trivial. We will say that G (resp. Tr G) is genuinely D-partite whenever no two colors are parallel in G. Equivalently, G

(resp. Tr G) is genuinely D-partite whenever, for some #» σ “ p σ1, . . . , σ D q P SDkpGqpGq, the permutations σ1, . . . , σ D

are pairwise disjoint. 

Clearly, the second formulation of the second definition does not depend on the choice of representative #» σ P SDkpGqpGq

and is therefore consistent. Note also that, when D “ 2, G is genuinely D-partite if and only if it is a union of cyclic graphs Ck1 , . . . , C kp , with at least one ki larger or equal to 2. According to the following proposition, any genuinely D-partite trace-invariant can be used to characterize separable states: more precisely, the modulus of such an invariant is maximized precisely on the orbit of separable states.                    

> 7Recall that, for any integer kě3, the k-purity of a mixed state ρis defined to be Tr pρkq, and that ρis pure ( i.e. rank one) if and only if Tr pρkq “ 1. Hence, if ρis the reduced density matrix of a bipartite state |ψy, then |ψyis separable if and only if Tr pρkq “ 1.

to HB2 b`HB1 b p HFqbp k´2q˘, and multi-indices associated with the tensor factor HB1 bp HFqbp k´2q are represented in blue. #» σ “ p σ1, . . . , σ D q P SDk pGq of pairwise disjoint permutations. In particular, one has σ1 ‰ σ2, meaning that there exists some k0 P t 1, . . . , k u such that σ1pk0q ‰ σ2pk0q. pid H1 b ρ2pσ2q b ¨ ¨ ¨ b ρD pσD qq | ψybk “ η |ψybk , (61) where η is a unit complex number. The right-hand side is manifestly separable in the bipartition pHFqbk “

HF b p HFqbk´1, hence the left-hand side must be too. A necessary condition for this to hold is that |ψy must be separable as a bipartite state in HF » HB1 bHB2 . Indeed, any entanglement for |ψy across the bipartition HB1 bHB2

would generate entanglement for pid H1 b ρ2pσ2q b ¨ ¨ ¨ b ρD pσD qq | ψybk across the bipartition HF b p HFqbk´1, as is made intuitively clear by Fig. 4 (and can be checked analytically by computing 2-purities). Hence, we can find states |ϕy P SpHB1 q and |ηy P SpHB2 q such that |ψy “ | ϕy b | ηy. We then have Tr Gp| ψyq “ Tr H1 p| ηyq Tr H2 p| ϕyq , (62) where H1 P G|B1| is genuinely |B1|-partite and H2 P G|B2| is genuinely |B2|-partite. More precisely, H1 is the |B1|-colored graph represented by the |B1|-tuple of permutations pσcqcPB1 (which are pairwise distinct); and, similarly, 17 H2 is the |B2|-colored graph encoded by pσcqcPB1 . By Lem. 2.4, | Tr Gp| ψyq| , | Tr H1 p| ηyq| and | Tr H2 p| ϕyq| are all bounded from above by 1. It results that | Tr Gp| ψyq| “ 1 if and only if: 

| Tr H1 p| ηyq| “ 1 and | Tr H2 p| ϕyq| “ 1 . (63) By the induction hypothesis, these conditions hold if and only if |ηy is separable in HB1 and |ϕy is separable in 

HB2 , which, together, are equivalent to |ψy being separable in HF “ HB1 b HB2 . This concludes the proof. 

Remark 2.19. Note that, if |ψy is a separable D-partite state, then Tr H p| ψyq “ 1 for any H P GD . Hence, with the hypotheses of the previous proposition, we have: 

| Tr Gp| ψyq| “ 1 ñ @H P GD , Tr H p| ψyq “ 1 . (64) 

Example 2.20. Let us illustrate the previous result in D “ 3. It is possible to assess whether a tripartite state

For arbitrary integers D ě 2 and k P N˚, it is clear that one can find a genuinely D-partite graph G P GD

with kpGq “ k if and only if |Sk| “ k! ě D. In particular, one can always take k “ D, as in the D “ 3 example just given. ## 2.6 Coarse-graining of D-partite state spaces 

In this subsection, we introduce natural coarse-graining maps relating D-partite state spaces to D1-partite state-spaces, with D1 ď D. When D1 ă D, this will allow us to distinguish multipartite entanglement properties which can be captured by effectively D1-partite invariants from those that can only be identified by genuinely D-partite invariants. 19 Genuinely D-partite entangled states. In Sec. 2.5, we have introduced the notion of genuinely D-partite invariants, which allow testing the separability of a state. In the existing literature, the notion of genuinely D-partite entangled state was also introduced, which we now recall (see e.g. Ref. [52]). 

Definition 2.21. Let D ě 2 and F a D-partite state space. A state |ψy P SpHFq is said to be genuinely D-partite entangled – or genuinely entangled on F – if, for any coarse-graining F1 ľ F with |F1| “ 2, |ψy is entangled in HF1

.

Genuinely D-partite states admit a useful characterization in terms of trace-invariants. 

Proposition 2.22. Let D ě 2, F “ p H1, . . . , HD q a D-partite state space, and |ψy P SpHFq. |ψy is genuinely entangled on F if and only if: 

@G P GD zGtriv  

> D

, | Tr Gp| ψyq| ă 1 . (72) 

Proof. Let us proceed by contraposition, and assume that |ψy is not genuinely entangled. Then we have a bipartition 

ζ “ p B1, B 2q such that |ψy is separable as a bipartite state on HFζ “ HB1 b HB2 . The 2-purity of the reduced density matrix on B1 is then equal 1, which provides a graph G P GD zGtriv  

> D

such that | Tr Gp| ψyq| “ 1. Reciprocally, suppose we are given a graph G P GD zGtriv  

> D

such that | Tr Gp| ψyq| “ 1. Then there exists a coarse-graining to a D1-partite state space with 2 ď D1 ď D, and a genuinely D1-partite graph G1 P GD1 such that 1 “ |Tr Gp| ψyq | “ |Tr G1 p| ψyq |. Indeed, such a G1 can be obtained from G by merging any maximal block of parallel colors in G into a single color. We are necessarily left with D1 ě 2 coarse-grained color labels after this is done, otherwise G would have to be in Gtriv  

> D

. By construction, the resulting graph G1 has no parallel colors left i.e. it is genuinely D1-partite. From Prop. 2.18, it follows that |ψy is separable on this coarse-graining, therefore it is not genuinely entangled on F.

Partially separable states. A D-partite state is said to be partially separable (see e.g. Refs. [53–56]) if it is separable relative to some coarse-graining of its D-partite state space (hence, as a D1-partite state with D1 ď D). In more detail, consider a pure state |ψy on F “ p H1, . . . , HD q, and let ζ be a partition of t1, . . . , D u. The state |ψy

is said to be ζ-separable if it is separable on the coarse-graining Fζ of F. We let ξ be the finest partition such that 

|ψy is ξ-separable. We then have |ψy “ â

> BPξ

|ψyB , with |ψyB P HB for any B P ξ. Furthermore, the (pure) state 

|ψyB induced on each block B of ξ is genuinely |B|-partite entangled . The partition ξ will be important in later sections. We also recall the following related concepts from the existing literature (see e.g. Refs. [57–59]). One says that |ψy is r-separable (resp. h-producible ) if there exists a partition ζ with # pζq “ r (resp. max  

> BPζ

|B| “ h) such that |ψy is ζ-separable. The smallest m such that |ψy is m-producible is called the entanglement depth of |ψy.Equivalently, m is the size of the largest block of the partition ξ introduced above. Similarly, the number of blocks of ξ, denoted # pξq, is the largest r such that |ψy is r-separable. Finally, note that a state |ψy is genuinely entangled on HF “ H1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b HD if and only if it is 1-separable (if and only if it is D-producible, in which case m “ D). These concepts can all be nicely reformulated in terms of trace-invariants.

While coarse-graining can render a partially separable state fully separable (it is precisely the definition of partial separability), it can also diminish the number of blocks of ξ (as introduced above): this happens when the coarse-graining identifies two colors in different blocks of ξ. This is illustrated for instance by the examples of Fig. 5 (which are all tensor products of Bell states): any of these four states is genuinely entangled on its native D-partite state space (with D “ 2, 3, 4 and 6, respectively), but it is not necessarily so on a fine-graining of this state space. In particular, for each of those examples, we can actually find a fine-graining of the state space relative to which the entanglement depth of the state is 2. 12 This reflects the fact that, in all those examples, entanglement is mediated by Bell states, which, in a natural sense, only contain bipartite entanglement. In certain situations, one is rather interested in characterizing a stronger notion of “genuine D-partite entanglement”, that is, one that cannot be reduced to entanglement between D1 parties with D1 ă D upon fine-graining. This can motivate the definition of new entanglement measures, such as the genuine multi-entropy introduced in Ref. [60], which have the property to vanish on products of Bell states. In a forthcoming paper, we will investigate further how this stronger notion of “genuine D-partite entanglement” can be quantified by means of trace-invariants. The notion of coarse-graining defined above naturally leads to the introduction of several equivalence relations that are coarser than full LU -equivalence. For instance, we can decide to analyse multipartite entanglement through the (limited) lens of bipartite entanglement, by comparing multipartite states in terms of the entanglement properties of all their bipartitions.

This characterization is easily transcribed in terms of trace-invariants. For this purpose, let us introduce the notion of cyclic graph . Let G P GD . We say that G is cyclic if there exists a bipartition ζ of t1, . . . , D u such that G P GD,ζ . Equivalently, G is cyclic if there is a permutation τ P SkpGq such that G can be represented by a D-tuple of permutations pσ1, . . . , σ D q obeying: for any c “ t 1, . . . , D u, σc P t id , τ u. Trivial graphs ( i.e. graphs from Gtriv  

> D

) are cyclic. Any other cyclic graph is one for which the color set t1, . . . , D u can be partitioned into two non-trivial subsets; any two edges incident to the same vertex in G are then required to be parallel if and only if their color belong to the same subset of the color partition. It is not difficult to see that a G P GD is a connected 

cyclic graph if and only if it can be represented as above with τ “ p 1 2 ¨ ¨ ¨ Dq a full cycle. This includes so-called 

necklace graphs (see Sec. 3.1.1). We then have the following characterization.

xTr Gp| ψyqy “ x Tr Gp| ϕyqy . (79) 22 2.7 Entanglement monotones from trace-invariants 

We will now illustrate how trace-invariants can be used to investigate order relations in operational resource theories of entanglement such as LO (Local Operations), LOCC (Local Operations and Classical Communication), or LOSR 

(Local Operations and Shared Randomness). We will focus on the common layer of such operational theories, namely LO , and only discuss relations between deterministic pure states. Furthermore, we will limit our analysis to the most elementary family of LO monotones one can construct from trace-invariants, which we may interpret as multipartite generalizations of R´ enyi entanglement entropies (see Ex. 2.35 below). Other interesting families of monotones will be introduced and investigated in a forthcoming paper by the same authors. The orbit of separable states, which we can interpret as the orbit of least entangled states in LO (and in any other operational resource theory of entanglement) has already been characterized by means of trace-invariants: by Prop. 2.18, a D-partite state |ψy is separable if and only if | Tr Gp| ψyq| “ 1 for any G P Gc 

> D

. This raises the following natural question: are there restrictions on how the quantities t| Tr Gp| ψyq|u GPGc 

> D

into another pure state via a LO transformation? Let us find out. Given two finite-dimensional D-partite state spaces F “ p H1, . . . , HD q and F1 “ p H1

> 1

operations is the same as the preorder induced by LOSR operations (see Ref. [62]). The two resource theories do differ for mixed states. Thanks to Stinespring’s theorem, a quantum operation can always be implemented by a unitary transformation on a larger system including ancilla degrees of freedom, followed by a trace over the ancilla Hilbert space. Combined with Prop. 2.8, this allows us to characterize the LO preorder in terms of trace-invariants.

Combined with the degree bound from Prop. 2.11, this leads to a refinement of the previous proposition, in which only trace-invariants with degree smaller than some finite maximal degree need to be examined. Since it will be of little use for us in the present paper, we leave the determination of the exact value of this bound as an exercise for the interested reader.

Remark 2.31. In Ref. [62] (Corollary 8), a necessary (but insufficient) condition for |ψy ÝÑ  

> LO

|ϕy was provided in the multipartite setting. In the language of Prop. 2.28, that condition is equivalent to the existence of a D-partite state |ηy such that, for any bipartite connected graph G, Tr Gp| ψyq “ Tr Gp| ϕyq Tr Gp| ηyq . Clearly, this condition can be equivalently expressed as relations involving the entanglement spectra of arbitrary bipartite coarse-grainings of the states |ψy, |ϕy, and |ηy. Such a formulation is analogous to that of Cor. 2.30, and it is in this form that this necessary condition was originally stated in Ref. [62]. A clear benefit of our reliance on trace-invariants instead of eigenvalues is that it allows for a complete characterization of the LO preorder, in the bipartite and multipartite settings alike.

It would be interesting to investigate the nature of the subvariety defined by the equation Tr PT 3 p| ψyq “ 0in concrete, low-dimensional examples of D-partite Hilbert spaces (such as the three-qutrit state space of |ψex y), building on insightful connections between algebraic geometry and the problem of entanglement classification (see 

e.g. [63] for a review of this approach). More broadly, Cor. 2.37 calls for a combinatorial characterization of trace-invariants that admit zeroes, and for an analysis of the subvarieties formed by these zeroes in low dimensions.

26 Remark 2.39. The situation described by Cor. 2.37 and illustrated in the context of a tripartite system in Ex. 2.38 cannot be realized in the bipartite setting. Indeed, given that reduced density matrices are positive definite, any bipartite trace-invariant is valued in R˚` and thus never vanishes. Hence, Cor. 2.37 captures a genuinely multipartite phenomenon.

LO preorder.

> LO

|ψ1y, which concludes the proof. 

# 3 Families of trace-invariants, reference states, Haar-random states 

In this section, we start by describing and reviewing the properties of particular families of trace-invariants which have appeared in the recent literature, emphasizing their combinatorial description in terms of colored graphs (which, despite its convenience, is not always explicit in the literature). We then introduce the particular families of reference states we will focus our attention on in later sections, including the Haar-random state, which is the only non-deterministic state we will investigate in detail in the present contribution. 

## 3.1 Families of trace-invariants under consideration 

3.1.1 Entanglement R´ enyi entropies and cyclic graphs 

Let k ą 1 be an integer. Given a pure state |ψy on a bipartite Hilbert space H1 b H2, the entanglement R´ enyi-k

entropy of |ψy is the R´ enyi-k entropy of the state ρ1 induced on H1 (resp. of ρ2 induced on H2). As already stated in Sec. 2.5, the associated trace-invariant - the k-purity of ρ1 (resp. of ρ2) - corresponds to a cyclic graph having 2k vertices, denoted by Ck and represented in Fig. 6: Tr Ck p| ψyq “ Tr ρk 

> 1

“ Tr ρk 

> 2

. (105) While the entanglement R´ enyi-k entropies are defined for k ě 2, we remark that Ck and the associated trace-invariants are defined for k ě 1. As already mentioned in the introduction and in Sec. 2, the knowledge of this quantity for arbitrary integer values of k characterizes fully the entanglement spectrum of |ψy, or equivalently, its entanglement structure. 

Figure 6: Family of two-colored graph Ck associated with k-purities of reduced density matrices in the bipartite setting. More generally, starting from a D-partite state space F “ p H1, . . . , HD q, with D ě 3, one can coarse-grain its Hilbert space HF “ H1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b HD into a 2-partite Hilbert space (see Sec. 2.6): choosing B Ĺ t 1, . . . , D u with

3.1.2 Melonic invariants 

The melonic family plays a central role in the study of random tensor models, particularly in the context of their 1{N expansion. In these models, the leading order of the expansion is dominated by melonic diagrams, which are specific Feynman graphs characterized by their tree-like series-parallel structure. This dominance has been proven in various settings, including colored tensor models (see Ref. [66]), multi-orientable models (see Ref. [67]), and uncolored tensor models (see Ref. [68]). The melonic family is significant due to its connection with the 

Sachdev–Ye–Kitaev (SYK) model (see Refs. [69, 70]), which has motivated their study as toy models for quantum gravity and holography (see Ref. [71]). Melonic graphs can be defined recursively. The only graph with k “ 1 – called the 2 -vertex graph and which represents the square norm of a tensor – is melonic. Starting from a 2-vertex graph, one constructs all connected melonic graphs by inductively performing the graph operation of Fig. 8 (referred to as a melonic insertion ). Non-connected melonic graphs have connected components constructed this way. An example of the construction of connected melonic graphs is depicted in Fig. 9. See Ref. [7] for more details on melonic graphs. 

Figure 8: Graph operation involved in the recursive definition of melonic graphs, called melonic insertion. 

Figure 9: Recursive construction of a melonic 3-colored graph starting from a 2-vertex graph (on the left). Finally, for c0 P t 1, . . . , D u and B Ă t 1, . . . , D u, we call c0-melonic graph (resp. B-melonic graph) a melonic graph for which the move of Fig. 8 is never performed on an edge of color c0 (resp. on any edge of color c P B). Equivalently, a melonic graph G is c0-melonic (resp. B-melonic) if and only if the graph Gxc0 obtained by removing all the edges of color c0 (resp. the graph G|B ) is connected. The following properties - which follow from the definition - will be useful later on: if G is c0-melonic, then 

Gxc0 is melonic, and if G is B-melonic, it is B1-melonic for any B1 Ă B. If a connected graph G is melonic but not c-melonic for any c (resp. B-melonic but not B1 melonic for any B1 Ę B), then for any c and any B1 Ę B, Gˆc

(resp. G|B1 ) is disconnected and melonic. Furthermore, adding an extra color D ` 1 to a connected and melonic G

to obtain G1 P Gc

> D`1

, then G1 is melonic if and only if it is pD ` 1q-melonic. For any color c P t 1, . . . , D u, we can consider the bipartition ζ “ tt cu, tcuu . Any element G P GD,ζ is then 

tcu-melonic, which we will abridge to c-melonic throughout the paper (see e.g. the leftmost graph of Fig. 7). By contrast, a cyclic graph associated with any other bipartition is not melonic. 28 3.1.3 Planar invariants 

Another kind of colored graphs (and corresponding trace-invariants) of interest are those with a planar regular embedding (see Ref. [72]) or jacket (see Refs. [73–75]). Given a connected graph, one may view it as embedded into a surface by choosing a cyclic ordering of its edges around each vertex; that is, a graph together with this additional structure can be seen as drawn on a two-dimensional oriented surface (up to isomorphisms): the cyclic ordering corresponds to the list of edges encountered going around each vertex on the surface. Such an embedded graph may equivalently be interpreted as a ribbon graph or a combinatorial map (see e.g. [76, 77]). In particular, given a connected D-colored graph G P Gc 

> D

and a cyclic ordering of the color-set t1, . . . , D u, which we prescribe in the form of a permutation τ P SD comprised of a single cycle of length D, we can cyclically orient the edges around the black (resp. white) vertices according to 

τ (resp. τ ´1). The embedded graphs obtained from G for different choices of τ are its regular embeddings tGτ u

(see Ref. [72]), also called jackets (see Refs. [73–75]) of G. Note also that Gτ “ Gτ ´1 for any τ , so that G admits  

> pd´1q!2

regular embeddings (there exist other embeddings of G, which are obtained by allowing vertex-dependent color orderings). The genus gpGτ q P N is the number of holes of the underlying orientable surface. We introduce the notation 

gτ pGq “ gpGτ q (106) Remark that gτ pGq “ gτ ´1 pGq and that if gτ pGq “ 0 for some τ , the corresponding embedding of G is planar (the underlying surface is a sphere). Moreover, a graph is melonic if and only if all its regular embeddings are planar (see Refs. [7, 8, 75]). Trace-invariants associated with colored graphs with one or more planar jackets will be relevant in Sec. 4. 

3.1.4 Maximally single-trace invariants 

Maximally single-trace colored graphs were introduced as a distinguished class of interactions in tensorial quan-tum field theories, motivated in particular by strongly coupled models such as the SYK model and other related holographic models (see Refs. [47, 78]). A maximally single-trace D-colored graph G is connected and satisfies the properties that for any pair of colors pi, j q P t 1, . . . , D u2, the restriction of the graph G to edges labeled by colors i, and j has only one connected component. Said differently, this means that removing any subset of colors 

C Ă t 1, . . . , D u, with 0 ď |C| ď D ´ 2, from a maximally single-trace invariant leaves the graph connected. While maximally single-trace graphs do not exist for arbitrary order k,17 it is known that for any number D of colors there exist maximally single-trace D-colored graphs (see proof in Ref. [47]). In Fig. 10, we show particular examples of maximally single-trace colored graphs with a varying number of colors. As a remark, we observe that certain edge-colorings of some cage graphs such as the Heawood graph (see the leftmost graph of Fig. 10 and Ref. [79]) and the Balaban 10-cage graph (see the middle-left graph of Fig. 10 and Ref. [80]) are maximally single-trace. 

Figure 10: Examples of maximally single-trace invariants. From left to right: an edge-coloring of the Heawood graph which is maximally single-trace; an edge-coloring of the Balaban 10-cage graph which is maximally single-trace; a 

D “ 4 example of a maximally single-trace invariant; and the 5-colored complete bipartite graph K 5,5.In the following subsection, we present a distinguished family of trace-invariants arising in quantum information theory: the moments of the partial transpose, whose odd moments are maximally single-trace.    

> 17 An exception stands for D“2 since all connected graphs are maximally single-trace.

## 3.2 Quantum states under consideration 

In this section, we introduce an infinite family of deterministic states, which have a modular structure that can be conveniently encoded into graphs, and that we will refer to as reference states . We also recall the definition and main properties of a random Haar-distributed state. Both types of states will serve as benchmarks to assess the distinguishing power of various families of trace-invariants in later sections.

Equivalence relations on reference states. The weighted multiset M associated with a reference state ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D

still carries redundant information if one only cares about the LU Fζ -orbit of ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D. It is convenient for this purpose to make use of two standard notions from the formalism of multisets. First, there is the notion of sum of multisets :denoted `, it is the symmetric binary operation which, to any multisets M “ t x1, . . . , x pu, M1 “ t y1, . . . , y q u,associates the multiset M ` M1 “ t x1, . . . , x p, y 1, . . . , y q u. In other words, ` is a kind of multiset version of the notion of union of sets, with respect to which the multiplicity of any element is additive. We can also define the 

support of a multiset : if C is a multiset, we will denote by Supp pCq the ordinary set consisting of all the color labels 

c that occur at least once in C. We will make use of this concept in relation to multisets of colors; for instance, if C “ t 1, 1, 1, 3, 3, 5u, then Supp pCq “ t 1, 3, 5u. With these notions at hand, we can introduce four independent equivalence conditions on the multisets representing reference states. First of all, any separable state appearing as a tensor factor of ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D can be omitted. In other words, if we can find a weighted multiset M1, a color c P t 1, . . . , D u

and some integer N P N˚ such that M “ M1 ` t cuN , then M and M1 will represent the same D-partite LU orbit in 

Fζ ; hence, we will write: 

M “ M1 ` t cuN ñ M „ M1 . (140) For instance, for the reference state introduced in (139), we have tt 1, 1, 2u4, t2u3u „ tt 1, 1, 2u4u. In graphical terms, any univalent hyper-edge can be deleted. Furthermore, for any B Ă t 1, . . . , D u with |B| ě 2, a tensor factor of the form |ψyB, 1 is itself a separable state, which leads to a second equivalence condition:

Canonical representation of reference states. Equations (140), (141), (142) and (143) define an equivalence relation „ on the set of multisets representing reference states (or, equivalently, on their associated graphs). Com-bining these relations, it is then clear than, given a reference state ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D, one can always find a multiset M

representing it that obeys the following additional conditions: for any block Cw Ă t 1, . . . , D u appearing in M, one must have 1) |C| ě 2; 2) w ě 2; 3) C “ Supp pCq (i.e. C does not contain repeated colors); 4) there is no other block of the form Cw1 with w1 P N˚ appearing in M. In graphical terms, such a multiset can be represented by a hypergraph having: 1) no hyper-edge connecting a single vertex; 2) no hyper-edge of weight 1; 3) no repeated half-edge in any hyper-edge; 4) no repeated hyper-edge. It is also clear that such a multiset (resp. graph) is unique, and we will refer to it as the canonical multiset (resp. canonical hypergraph ) representing ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D (resp. pπ, w q). For example, the canonical weighted multiset representing the weighted partition pπ0, w 0q introduced in (138) is 

tt 1, 2, 6u2, t5, 6u3, t3, 4u6u , (144) and its associated canonical graph is represented in Fig. 25. The data of a canonical multiset M can be equivalently (and more conveniently) encoded into a weight function αM : tB Ă t 1, . . . , D u | | B| ě 2u Ñ N˚, defined as follows. For any block B Ă t 1, . . . , D u involving at least two subsystems, αMpBq denotes the weight assigned to B if B

40 appears in M (in which case αMpBq ě 2), and is set to αpBq “ 1 whenever B does not occur in M. For any weight function α : tB Ă t 1, . . . , D u | | B| ě 2u Ñ N˚, let us define the D-partite state

Partial separability and related notions. Consider a reference state ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D and consider an hypergraph representation of that state obeying the following condition: none of its hyper-edges of valency 2 or higher have weight 1 ( i.e. we forbid the use of hyper-edges of weight 1 to represent the inclusion of a trivial GHZ state on two or more subsystems). This condition is in particular obeyed if we choose the canonical hypergraph representation of ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D. Let ξ be the partition of t1, . . . , D u given by the connected components of the hypergraph representing ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D. On each connected component, the state is genuinely entangled: it is the finest ζ such that ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D is 

ζ-entangled (see Sec. 2.6). Then # pξq is the maximum r for which ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D is r-separable. If m is the number 41 of colored vertices in the largest connected component of the hypergraph representing ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D (that is, m is the maximum size of blocks of ξ), then m is the entanglement depth of ˇˇΨpπ,w q

D.Graphically, coarse-graining amounts to the following: merging Hc and Hc1 to Hc2 “ Hc b Hc1 amounts to merging the vertices of color c and c1 in the graphical representation of the hyper-edges. Conversely, fine-graining the system by splitting Hc2 “ Hc1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b HcI into Hc “ Hc1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b Hcs and Hc1 “ Hcs`1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b HcI amounts to splitting the colored vertex of color c2 in two colored vertices of colors c1 and c2, keeping the right hyper-edges attached to each one of the two resulting vertices. Note that this last procedure requires specifying the actual weighted partiton pπ, w q and not just the multisets, which is unavoidable since we are breaking the local unitary invariance to a subgroup in the process. We recover graphically the facts stated in Sec. 2.6: coarse-graining and fine-graining may respectively diminish and raise the number of connected components of ξ, thereby changing the partial separability properties. The states represented in (a), (b) (resp. (c), (d)) in Fig. 26 are obtained by coarse graining (resp. fine graining 27 ) of the central state ˇˇΨpπ1,w 1q

D associated with the weighted partition 

pπ1, w 1q “ tt 11, 12, 21u3, t22u2, t31, 32u5, t41, 52u6, t42, 51u7, t61u2, t62u4u , (149) and whose entanglement depth m and maximal r of r-separability respectively take the values 2 and 4. For the other 4 states, these values are respectively: (a) 1 and 3; (b) 3 and 1; (c) and (d) 3 and 7 (but the partition ξ

differs).

## 3.3 LU -classification of reference states 

Let D ě 2. The goal of this subsection is to establish a property we already alluded to, namely: that the weight function α associated with a reference state |ϕαy (as defined in (145)) labels the LU -equivalence classes of reference states. A first hint that this could be the case is that this description of reference states is stable under coarse-graining. Figure 27: Examples of LU (in)equivalence relations between reference states. This illustrates both the graphical equivalence relations introduced in Sec. 3.2.1 (Eqs. (140)–(143)), and the LU -classification result of Thm. 3.3. Thm. 3.3 is illustrated in Fig. 27 for 3-partite reference states. This result confirms that weight functions label the LU -classes of reference states. As a result, the four elementary equivalence relations Eqs. (140)–(143) introduced in Sec. 3.2.1 allow us to explore all the multisets representing a given LU -class of reference states. Equivalently, 45 the set of decorated hypergraphs representing a given LU -class of reference states can be explored by means of four elementary graphical moves: 1) the addition or removal of an univalent hyper-edge of arbitrary weight; 2) the addition or removal of an hyper-edge of weight 1; 3) the graphical move illustrated in Fig. 23; 4) the graphical move illustrated in Fig. 24. In the following, we will always rely on graphical representations of reference states that do not feature hyper-edges of weight 1 (since those carry no information and can always be suppressed by means of graphical moves of type 2)). In that case, all the LU -equivalent reference states generated by the graphical moves 1), 3) and 4) induce the same ξ, defined as the partition of t1, . . . , D u induced by the connected components of the hypergraph representing the states. The converse is not true, however. Point 2 of Thm. 3.3 states that it is sufficient to consider the trace-invariants corresponding to ME Dn for any

Here are some comments on this problem for small values of D.– For D “ 2, we have seen (in Lem. 3.2) that it is sufficient to consider C2 (see Fig. 6), which is in fact equal to ME 22 (so that Lem. 3.2 can be understood as a D “ 2 version of Thm. 3.3). – For D “ 3, Thm. 3.3 states that it is sufficient to consider: ME 32, which corresponds to RM 4 (see Fig. 22); ME 33,shown on the left of Fig. 18; RME pBq

This is too much information: it is actually sufficient to consider RM 4, ME 33, together with any two connected graphs with k “ 2. Alternatively, if one wishes to rely on the simplest possible graphs, one may instead consider PT 3 (left of Fig. 12), for which Eq. (162) reads 2 pxt1,2,3u `xt1,2u `xt1,3u `xt2,3uq “ 0, and the three connected graphs with k “ 2, which give: xt1,2,3u ` xt1,2u ` xt2,3u “ 0 (together with two independent equations obtained by cyclic permutation of the color labels). – We expect that for D ą 3, there always exists a simpler set of invariants playing the role of the set introduced in Point 2 of Thm. 3.3: indeed, our proof only relies on the cancellation of constant terms in polynomials that may have degree up to D ´ 1, and we should therefore not expect it to be optimal (if anything, we might expect it to be less and less optimal as D grows). ## 3.4 Asymptotic LU -equivalences and LU -equivalences in scaling 

We conclude by discussing a couple of examples of asymptotic LU -relations (as defined in Sec. 2.4) that involve reference states. To begin with, it was recently established that the correlation functions of Gaussian random tensors of order 

D ě 3 fail to factorize in the same way as those of Gaussian (or Haar-distributed) random matrices in the large 

N limit (see Ref. [31]). This result has a straightforward but interesting implication in our context: while a Haar-random bipartite quantum state can be well-approximated by a deterministic state in the large N limit (namely, a maximally entangled Bell state, with flat entanglement spectrum, as first observed by Page in Ref. [29]), a similar result is provably impossible to achieve in the multipartite setting. 47 Proposition 3.5. Let D ě 2. For every N P N˚, let |φN y denote the Haar-distributed random D-partite pure state on pCN qbD , and let |Bell N y denote a maximally entangled bipartite pure state on pCN qb2.1. (193) In the following section, we will focus on specific examples of reference states, defined on D-partite state spaces with equally sized parties and equally sized fine-grained building blocks. We will see that the evaluation of a trace-invariant G on such a state is fully encoded into the asymptotic scaling sG introduced in Sec. 2.4. As a result, we will see that, for such states, the problem of state discrimination can be approached by purely combinatorial methods. More precisely, we will find out that it can be related in a precise manner to properties of certain graph-theoretic combinatorial quantities, some of which have already been extensively studied in the literature (in most cases, for different purposes). In addition, given that our combinatorial analysis will rely solely on the values of asymptotic scaling parameters tsGuGPGc 

> D

, any statement regarding the exact state discrimination of a pair of reference states implies a statement regarding the asymptotic state discrimination of whole families of states (that are LU -equivalent in scaling to one of those two reference states). For instance, coming back to Ex. 3.7: if the scaling sG of a graph 

G discriminates between |GHZ yB,N and |GHZ yB,N 2 , then the graph G can also be used to discriminate the states 

|ρ1yB,N and |ρ2yB,N 2 .

# 4 Distinguishing power of trace-invariants for a subset of reference states 

In this section, we investigate the ability of trace invariants to discriminate between a subset of reference quantum states and Haar-random states (at leading order, in the latter case). Our goal is to characterize the classes of trace-invariants for which such distinctions are effective or, conversely, fail to hold. 

## 4.1 Some combinatorial quantities responsible for state discrimination 

Let us come back to the fine-grained definition of reference states from the beginning of Sec. 3.2.1. We have a 

D-partite state space H1 b ¨ ¨ ¨ b HD (with D ě 2), and each tensor factor Hc is itself divided into Ic subsystems 

tHpc,i qu1ďiďIc . From the remainder of the present section, we will make the following simplifying assumptions: a) that all D local subsystems have the same dimension N ě 2, and b) that they are subdivided into equally sized 49 elementary building blocks. Calling I the number of elementary building blocks in each local subsystem, we thus have: for any color c P t 1, . . . , D u,

|Ic| “ I , dim pHcq “ N , and @i P t 1, . . . , Iu , dim pHpi,c qq “ N 1{I def 

“ Ns . (194) We will fix I and consider N as a free parameter, conditioned on Ns “ N 1{I being an integer. For G to discriminate between the two states, the scalings must differ. sGp| GHZ |B yq ´ sGp| Φτ yq “ r sGp| GHZ |B yq ´ sGp| GHZ yqs ` r sGp| GHZ yq ´ sGp| Φτ yqs “ KB pGq ` gτ pGq ě 0 . (237) If the terms in the sum have the same sign, it results again in a positivity condition: G distinguishes |GHZ |B y and 

|Φτ y if and only if either KB pGq ą 0 or gτ pGq ą 0. This is not always the case: for instance, when comparing the states |GHZ |B y and |GHZ |B1 y, one may find graphs with KB pGq ą KB1 pGq, and conversely. This will have consequences in Sec. 5. 

Remark 4.2. Some of these quantities are well-known in the literature on random matrices and tensors. The genus of 3-colored graphs is the quantity involved in the 1{N expansion of two-matrix models, such as those describing the Ising model on random surfaces (see e.g. Refs. [102–104]), or three-matrix models, such as in Ref. [7]. The Gurau degree ω2 or the degree of compatibility ∆ play the same role regarding the 1{N expansion of random tensor models, see Refs. [7, 48, 66, 75, 105]. The c-degree Ωc governs the 1{N expansion of the SYK model, see Ref. [71]. In the same way as for the genus, most of the quantities introduced here have a geometric interpretation: D-colored graphs are dual to certain pD ´ 1q-dimensional triangulations, and the restrictions of the graphs to different subsets of colors count the number of lower-dimensional simplices of different kinds. The jackets are related to so-called Heegaard splittings, and so on. See Refs. [74, 106] for more details. The κppq, introduced in Eq. (213) , then count the number fD´p´1 of pD ´ p ´ 1q-dimensional simplices of the triangulation (and f´1 is the number of connected components). Recall that xC pα, β q was introduced in Eq. (162) .Setting xC pα, β q “ xp for all C Ă t 1, . . . , D u satisfying |C| “ p ą 1, N P N˚, and introducing x0, x 1 P R, one can rewrite the scaling difference as 

psGp| ψβ yq ´ sGp| ψαyqq ¨ ln N “
