Producing graphs satisfying these conditions. Thm. 3.3 provides a set of trace-invariants whose knowledge allows determining the LU -equivalence class of a reference state. As discussed at the end of Sec. 3.3, for fixed D and for some fixed sub-family of LU -inequivalent reference states, one may wonder whether there exists a simpler set of trace-invariants that indeed take a different set of values for these states. Considering for instance the reference states |GHZ y, |GHZ |B y, |ϕpy, |Φτ y and |φy introduced in this subsection, the question is whether one can produce a simpler set of graphs G satisfying all the conditions listed in the tables above. 55 In the tripartite case, Fig. 30 shows such a graph 34 G P G3. Choosing the same labels for the black and white vertices linked by an edge of color 1, the permutations describing this example are 

σ1 “ id σ2 “ p 1 2 3 4 5 6 7 qp 8 9 q σ3 “ p 1 4 qp 2 5 9 qp 3 5 qp 6 8 q . (240) Recalling the definition of μG in Eq. (227), the resulting graph has the following properties: 

kpGq “ 9 , F12 pGq “ 2 , F13 pGq “ 4 , F23 pGq “ 3 , gpGq “ 1 , ∆pGq “ 2 , μGp| φyq “ 3 .35 (241) Already at D “ 4, numerically computing an example of a 4-colored graph satisfying all distinction conditions is out of reach. Indeed, the requirement that all combinatorial quantities be well-defined, together with the condition that the faces and connected components of subgraphs are pairwise disjoint, implies kpGq ě 21. Moreover, excluding the conditions on the Haar-random states, there are 70 constraints to fulfill. In practice, an exhaustive numerical search would involve verifying more than 2 .07 ˆ 10 42 quadruples of permutations. Furthermore, computing ∆ (Eq. (236)) requires computing ∆ ν pGq for all ν, which entails evaluating approximately 5 .11 ˆ 10 19 possibilities. 

Figure 30: Example of a 3-colored graph satisfying all the distinction conditions summarized in the tables. For a finite set of LU -inequivalent reference states, we expect that a D-colored graph with k white vertices chosen uniformly at random will satisfy the required distinction conditions with high probability when k is sufficiently large. It would be interesting to rigorously prove such expectation. 

## 4.2 Graph structure of distinguishing invariants 

The conditions listed in Sec. 4.1 for discriminating the specific examples of LU -inequivalent reference states intro-duced in that section involve several non-negative combinatorial quantities. For a given graph, one can compute these quantities to verify whether the conditions are satisfied, but it is harder to produce a graph satisfying this or that condition, as discussed at the end of Sec. 4.1. For some examples of reference states, one actually knows the structure of graphs for which the corresponding conditions are satisfied or not, and in some cases, one knows the structure of graphs for which the corresponding combinatorial quantities take a given specific value. The present subsection aims to review these situations. As a first example, it is trivial to classify the graphs according to the value taken by sGp| GHZ yq “ κpGq ´ kpGq,which is the non-positive quantity responsible for discrimination between |GHZ y and a separable state. 

Graphs of fixed KB . A second trivial example is given by KB pGq “ κpG|B q ´ κpGq, where B Ă t 1, . . . , D u: the graphs of vanishing KB - which do not distinguish between |GHZ |B y and |GHZ y on all parties - are again collections of 2-vertex graphs. Graphs of positive KB are relevant for the discrimination of a number of the reference states considered in Sec. 4.1. To construct all the graphs with a given fixed value of KB P r 0, k ´ 1s, one considers the 

|B|-colored graphs whose edges are colored according to the edges in B and with κpG|B q connected components, and then connect them using edges of the remaining colors to reduce the number of connected components to κ.A graph G for which κpG|B q “ κpG|B1 q (that is, KB “ KB1 ) is unable to distinguish the states |GHZ |B y and 

|GHZ |B1 y: distinguishing GHZ states shared by different parties requires non-symmetric graphs.

Another simple example is given by sGp| GHZ |B yq “ κpG|B q ´ kpGq,the non-positive quantity responsible for discrimination between |GHZ |B y and a separable state. The graphs G for which it takes a specific value x ď 0 are those for which G|B has k ` x connected components, while the edges of G

whose colors are not in B may be in any positions. More generally, we may consider the “fine-grained states” for which I “ 1 and π is directly a partition of t1, . . . , D u (the subsystems Hc are not subdivided). with |B| ą 1, we know how to construct the graphs with k fixed and whose edges are colored according to B, and for which the tsGp| GHZ |B yqu B take some fixed values that sum to sG. One may then choose a labeling of the white vertices from 1 to k for each B, and merge the vertices with the same label. If B “ t cu, the color-c edges may be in any position. The graphs constructed this way are all the graphs with the desired value sG. sG are those for which G P GD,π (since ξ “ π for these states). 

Genus of a jacket. For any D and any cyclic permutation τ P SD , it is possible to build (and count) the graphs 

G for which gτ pGq takes some fixed value. It is indeed possible to show 36 that D-colored graphs G with gτ pGq “ g

fixed are in bijection with so-called pD ´ 1q-constellations of same genus g. They are specific kinds of graphs drawn on surfaces, introduced for counting branched coverings of the 2-sphere. There is a literature in on how to build and count constellations of fixed genus: see Ref. [110] for the planar case, and for instance in Ref. [111] for higher genus. 

Gurau degree ω2. For D “ 3, ω2 is equal to twice the genus of any of the two jackets (Eq. (208)): this case is treated in the previous paragraph. The p-complete degrees. From Thm. 4.3, the graphs of vanishing ωp are planar for D “ 3 and melonic for 

D ą 3. We can generate the graphs of fixed positive ωD´1 using Rem. B.6 in App. B. We have not studied the structure of graphs of fixed positive ωp for 2 ă p ă D ´ 1. Regarding graphs of vanishing ωpqq 

> p

: from Eq. (218) and Thm. 4.3, they are such that q-colored subgraphs are melonic. An example is given in Footnote 37 for q “ D ´ 1. This is the only characterization we have at this stage. 

Graphs with few faces. The quantity Fc ´ p D ´ 1qκ arising in Eq. (220) when comparing the scalings of |Φcy

and |GHZ y vanishes if and only if there is a single face for each pair of color pc, c 1q, c1 ‰ c, and fixed positive values are obtained distributing the numbers of faces. All moments of the partial transpose satisfy this condition for one color. Imposing the condition for all c, one obtains graphs with minimal F , called maximally single trace (see Sec. 3 and Refs. [47, 113]). Complete graphs and bipartite complete graphs are examples of such graphs. Only the partial transpose moments for odd n satisfy this condition. As explained in Ref. [47], one can build new examples from known ones by one of the binary operations that we will study in Sec. 6. We do not know of any complete characterization of the graphs with minimal Fc or minimal F .

The c-degree Ωc. This quantity arises in Eq. (222) when comparing Φ c and a lower dimensional GHZ state

Lemma 4.4. A 3-colored graph G is planar and compatible if and only if it is melonic. Proof. Melonic graphs are planar and compatible. Reciprocally, from the characterization of compatible graphs in Eq. (249) together with Eq. (208), if G is planar and compatible, then there is a pG P G4pGq such that Ω 0p pGq “ 0, so that pG is 0-melonic, and therefore G is melonic. 

## 4.3 Combinatorial quantities and trace-invariants from the literature 

The tables below review whether the combinatorial quantities introduced in Sec. 4.1 vanish or not for the trace-invariants presented in Sec. 3. For the following tables, we will need the values of ∆ for RM p1,2q

which are deduced from Ref. [114], 39 see also Ref. [71] and Ref. [37, 96, 97]. The compatibility of the odd and even moments of the partial transpose was proved in Ref. [35]. Concerning the multi-entropies, we will use the following lemma. 

Lemma 4.5. For n ą 1, the graphs ME Dn corresponding to the multi-entropies are incompatible. Proof. For ME Dn to be compatible, there must exist a way to add color-0 edges to obtain a pD ` 1q-colored graph 

zME Dn , such that the color-0 edges form non-crossing pairings on every face of colors pi, j q for 1 ď i ă j ď D.If the pairing is chosen to be non-crossing on the faces of colors p1, 2q and p1, 3q, then, by construction of the multi-entropies, the color-0 edges must be parallel to the color-1 edges. This configuration, however, fails to be non-crossing on the faces of colors p2, 3q.Finally, for D ě 4 the non-melonic D-colored cyclic graphs are incompatible due to the following lemma: 

Lemma 4.6. Let D ě 4. We consider a D-colored graph G and denote by V pGq its set of vertices. If there exists 

v P V pGq, such that v is on two different faces of size two (see Fig. 32), then the graph is incompatible. Proof. Assume G is compatible. The edges of color 0 of pG P MpGq must be non-crossing on each face since all Gromov products are vanishing. In particular, it cannot simultaneously be the case for both faces of size two being attached to v, since the non-crossing requirement would require pairing both faces. Hence, G is incompatible.

We now focus on the families of trace-invariants presented in Sec. 2 that admit a quantity defined by a limit, namely entanglement entropies, the partially transposed entropy, the logarithmic negativity, the reflected entropy, and the multi-entropy. Throughout, expectation values are evaluated at leading order in the large-N expansion and without considering analytical continuation (the limit as n or k approaches one is taken “naively”). Moreover, for each family of invariants, we assume that the logarithm and the averaging operation can be interchanged, which relies, with high probability, on the validity of the large-N factorization. 64 As we have seen in this section, the various combinatorial quantities associated to a D-colored graph G determine which features of the multipartite entanglement structure of reference states the invariant Tr G is able to reveal. In other words, the combinatorial quantities shed light on the structural properties of colored graphs (and trace-invariants) that enable the separation of LU -inequivalent reference states. In particular, they demonstrate that R´ enyi multi-entropies can distinguish the global structure of reference states, but not subsystems. Indeed, while Tr ME Dn p| GHZ B yq ‰ Tr ME Dn p| GHZ B1 yq for |B| ‰ |B1|, we have Tr ME Dn p| GHZ B yq “ Tr ME Dn p| GHZ B1 yq whenever |B| “

|B1|. Furthermore, a naive analytic continuation seems to suggest that, at leading order in the large-N limit, multi-entropies do not distinguish a Haar-random state from a GHZ state. 54 Although multi-entropies are prevalent in the literature (see e.g. Refs. [38, 40, 60, 61, 93–95]), it would be interesting to perform a precise analytic continuation to confirm this statement and, if necessary, compute the next-to-leading order correction in the large dimension limit. Regarding R´ enyi reflected multi-entropies, the same conclusion as for R´ enyi multi-entropies holds, except that the former can make a partial distinction about the subsystem. In the next section, we will focus on LO and 

LOCC transformations. We will see how the nonnegative property of combinatorial quantities clarifies the potential relations between a subset of reference states considered in this section. Subsequently, the use of trace-invariants will yield precise statements about the LO and LOCC transformations between reference states. 

# 5 Determining LO and LOCC relations between reference states by means of trace-invariants 

In general, addressing the existence of LO or LOCC maps between pairs of quantum states is a difficult problem. We will first show that combinatorial quantities provide a useful tool to rule out the possibility of certain LO maps between the subset of reference states considered Sec. 4. More generally, trace-invariants can be employed to obtain concrete results in the classification of LO transformations between reference states. Finally, this section concludes with a preliminary investigation of LOCC maps between reference states, by recalling and applying known LOCC 

protocols to these states. 

## 5.1 First combinatorial constraints on LO relations between reference states 

Let us start by gathering some partial information about LO preorder relations among reference states; a complete characterization will be provided in the next subsection. Introducing combinatorial quantities that characterize the subset of reference states investigated in Sec. 4 enables us to use their known properties to readily exclude the existence of LO transformations between most of those states, thereby highlighting their practical relevance. 

Proposition 5.1. Let D ě 2, B Ă t 1, . . . , D u with 2 ď | B| ď D ´ 1, and τ P SD a cyclic permutation. Except for the following potentially non-excluded LO transformations when D ě 3,

One can exclude the LO transformations from (2) |GHZ y or |GHZ |B y to |ΦB y, |Φτ y or |ϕpy using the same argument. Moreover, the existence for any D of a genuinely D-partite maximally single trace colored graph (see Sec. 3), which satisfies KB “ 0 for all subsets B, rules out the existence of a LO transformation from: (3) |GHZ |B y or 

|GHZ y to |GHZ |B1 y; and (4) from |ΦB y to either |GHZ y, |GHZ |B1 y or |ΦB1 y. From genuinely D-partite melonic graphs, we further deduce that: (5) |Φτ y and |ϕpy cannot be transformed to |GHZ y by LO , as well as (6) |Φτ y

transformation nor allow one to rule it out. Nevertheless, by exploiting the properties of trace-invariants in relation to the characterization of the LO preorder given in Prop. 2.28, one can recover a complete classification of LO 

relations amongst reference states, which is the purpose of the next subsection. 

## 5.2 Complete characterization of the LO preorder on reference states 

To address the characterization problem, we now turn our attention to reference states in general (as introduced in Sec. 3.2.1), which we will parametrize by their weight functions (as introduced in Eq. (145)). In doing so, we will show that a judicious use of trace-invariants allows for a complete characterization of all LO transformations among reference states, for any number of parties D. This will, in particular, enable us to answer the questions raised in the previous subsection, namely Eq. (257). To begin with, we introduce an order relation on the set of weight functions labeling reference states. 66 Definition 5.2. Let D ě 2, α : tB Ă t 1, . . . , D u | | B| ě 2u Ñ N˚ and β : tB Ă t 1, . . . , D u | | B| ě 2u Ñ N˚. We say that α divides β – noted α β – whenever: for any B Ă t 1, . . . , D u with |B| ě 2,

αpBq βpBq . (263) This defines a partial order on the set of weight functions. Our general theorem states that this partial order is, in fact, isomorphic to the LO partial order on the set of LU -classes of reference states.

Indeed, we first observe that if |B| ‰ p, there can be no LO map between |ϕpy and |GHZ |B y, as follows directly from Thm. 5.3. divides N 1{2), which is impossible except for the trivial case N “ 1. Thus, we can conclude the first corollary of Thm. 5.3: 

Corollary 5.5. There are no LO maps between the reference states |GHZ y, |GHZ |B y, |ΦB y, |Φτ y, and |ϕpy.

## 5.3 Towards a characterization of the LOCC preorder on reference states

Remark 5.8. When D “ 2, the LOCC preorder is characterized by majorization conditions on entanglement spectra (see Ref. [115]). In our setup, this result implies the equivalence relation:

In the remainder of this subsection, we illustrate how familiar LOCC protocols act on reference states, leaving the full characterization of the LOCC preorder on reference states as an open question for future work.

> pC,B qP EpHDq

LO transformation (see Cor. 2.33). Equivalently, the (generalized) R´ enyi entropies introduced as LO monotones in Exs. 2.34 and 2.35 are also non-decreasing under this special class of LOCC transformations. 

Quantum teleportation. The quantum teleportation protocol provides a mechanism by which a collection of GHZ states shared between a reference subsystem and each of the remaining subsystems can be merged into a single multipartite GHZ state using only LOCC operations. This mechanism extends naturally to the class of reference states considered in this paper.

Figure 40: Left: Penrose diagram illustrating the quantum teleportation protocol for five subsystems symbolized by the local observers Alice, Bob, Charlie, David, and Erin. The gold regions represent their respective laboratories into which one first identifies the dot in the bottom center of Alice’s laboratory as the prepared GHZ state in Hc, sep .Moreover, the measurement in Alice’s laboratory is illustrated by the LU transformations (namely, Xm1 , Xm2 , and the phase correction) it generates on B1 and B2. Finally, the LU -corrections applied by the local observers after the measurement (either Xm1 , Xm2 , or the phase correction for Alice) are depicted at the top of each laboratory. Since the LU -corrections for Bob, Charlie, David, and Erin depend on Alice’s measurement, classical communication is needed, proving diagrammatically that the protocol is indeed LOCC . Right: Conventions used in the picture that allow one to recover the GHZ state on B1 Y B2, i.e. on the system shared by Alice, Bob, Charlie, David, and Erin. Albeit more subtle than for Prop. 5.11, one may again verify that the LO monotonicity property of trace-invariants (Cor. 2.33) extends to the particular LOCC quantum teleportation protocol on reference states invoked in Prop. 5.13. As a matter of fact, assuming that the conditions of Prop. 5.13 are satisfied, i.e. |ψβ y ÝÑ  

> LOCC

|ψαy by quantum teleportation, one finds that for any colored graph G P GD

|Tr Gp| ψαyq ||Tr Gp| ψβ yq | “ N κpG|B q` kpGq´ κpG|B1 q´ κpG|B2 q ě 1 . (328) This is due to the following “triangle inequality” that holds for any colored graph G:

κpG|B q ě κpG|B1 q ` κpG|B2 q ´ kpGq . (329) 76 To see this, one can construct an abstract bipartite graph H whose white vertices (resp. black vertices) represent the connected components of G|B1 (resp. G|B2 ). In particular, the number of vertices of H is V pHq “ κpG|B1 q` κpG|B2 q.To define the edges of H, we note that any white vertex v of G belongs to a unique connected component C1 of 

G|B1 and a unique connected component C2 of G|B2 : we represent this situation by an edge connecting C1 and C2

in H. As a result of this construction, H has EpHq “ kpGq edges, and it is also clear that it is has κpHq “ κpG|B q

connected components. Henceforth, the quantity 

LpHq def 

“ EpHq ´ V pHq ` κpHq “ κpG|B q ´ p κpG|B1 q ` κpG|B2 qq ´ kpGq (330) computes the number of loops of the graph H, and is therefore non-negative. This yields Eq. (329). Finally, we note that the content of Prop. 5.13 can be conveniently visualized in the Hasse diagram HD . As highlighted by Fig. 41, the quantum teleportation protocol authorizes to move weights upstream in the Hasse diagram with B1, B2, B1 Y B2 and B1 X B2 forming a diamond. This is to be contrasted to the protocol of Prop. 5.13, which only allows us to move weights downstream of the Hasse diagram. At the moment, it is not clear to us how to efficiently describe the net effect of iterated quantum teleportation protocols at the level of the Hasse diagram (as we did in Prop. 5.13 for iterated applications of local measurement reductions). We leave this problem, as well as the broader question of how to fully characterize the LOCC preorder on reference states, open for future work.

# 6 Binary operation and tree construction of trace-invariants 

## 6.1 Binary operations 

This section aims to give a concrete way to compute and track the combinatorial quantities appearing in the distinction conditions imposed on trace-invariants. Indeed, computing the degree of compatibility and the other combinatorial quantities associated with a colored graph might be a challenging task. However, in the presence of specific binary operations denoted by 

O : GD ˆ GD ÝÑ GD (331) 

pG1, G 2q Þ ÝÑ G1OG2

the values of, e.g. ωppG1q, ∆pG1q, μ G1 and ωppG2q, ∆pG2q, μ G2 can be used directly to infer those of the combined object ωppG1 O G2q, ∆pG1 O G2q, μ G1 O G2 . This observation motivates the search for binary operations allowing for tractable expressions of those quantities. Among the most prominent examples of such operations are the union ,the flip , and the vertex contraction , which we now introduce. 

Definition 6.1. Let G1, G 2 P GD be two D-colored graphs having respectively kpG1q and kpG2q pairs of vertices. – Union. The disjoint union of G1 and G2 – denoted G1 \ G2 – is the D-colored graph whose connected components are exactly those of G1 and those of G2.61       

> 61 This definition is in accordance with the multiplicativity of the map HQGDÑTr Hunder disjoint unions, which was already commented on in Eq. (29).

77 – Flip. Let c P t 1, . . . , D u and eG1 (resp. eG2 ) be an edge of color c in G1 (resp. G2). Performing a flip on the pair of edges peG1 , e G2 q amounts to: a) cutting those two edges open in the graph G1 \ G2; and b) reconnecting the resulting half-edges in the only other way allowed by LU -invariance ( i.e. in a way that preserves the bipartite structure of the graph). The resulting D-colored graph is denoted G1 ˚peG1 ,e G2 q G2, and has kpG1q ` kpG2q

vertices. When the specific choice of pair of edges peG1 , e G2 q on which the flip is being performed is not relevant to the discussion, we will simply write G1 ˚ G2, or G1 ˚c G2 in case we want to keep track of the color of the edge being flipped. The operation is represented graphically in Fig. 42. 

Figure 42: Left: the graphs G1 and G2 with the edges eG1 and eG2 highlighted. Right: the result of the operation 

G1 ˚peG1 ,e G2 q G2.

– Vertex contraction. Let vG1 be a white (resp. black) vertex of G1 and vG2 a black (resp. white) vertex of 

G2. Contracting G1 and G2 along the pair pvG1 , v G2 q consists in: a) removing the vertex vG1 from G1 and the vertex vG2 from G2, and b) reconnecting the resulting half-edges in the only way that preserves the D-colored nature of the graph. This procedure yields a D-colored graph with kpG1q ` kpG2q ´ 1 vertices, denoted as 

G1 ˝pvG1 ,v G2 q G2. When the specific choice of pairs of vertices pvG1 , v G2 q is not essential, we will simply write 

G1 ˝ G2 to refer to the contraction operation in a general sense. This operation is illustrated in Fig. 43. 

Figure 43: Left: the graphs G1 and G2 with the vertices vG1 and vG2 highlighted. Right: the result of the operation 

G1 ˝pvG1 ,v G2 q G2.Given a set of operations O and explicit transformation rules for ∆ and μ, a natural strategy for constructing families of invariants is to consider tree-like compositions built recursively from a fixed set of generators. More precisely, let ApB, Oq denote the set of all graphs that can be generated by applying a collection of s binary operations O “ t O1, . . . , Osu to elements of a finite set of r invariants B “ t B1, . . . , B r u.Any element G P ApB, Oq can be obtained recursively given B P B, O P O and rG P ApB, Oq and the relation 

G “ rG O B . (332)

We study the binary operations presented in the last section, i.e. the union, the flip and the vertex contraction.

G is said to have only tree-like dominant graphs if any graph pG P GD`1pGq minimizing the degree of com-patibility while connecting the Gi’s (without necessarily connecting the connected components of the Gi’s) is tree-like on G.

(see Prop. D.6). Proof. See App. D. Determining whether a given graph has, exactly or not, tree-like dominant contributions is a difficult task. Nevertheless, the literature provides several families of graphs for which tree dominance is well understood, thus providing examples for which point 1 and point 2 of Thm. 6.4 can be applied. Indeed, in the tripartite case, it was shown in Ref. [118] that 3-colored planar graphs have only tree-like dominant graphs. |B| “ D ´ 2, have also only tree-like dominant graphs. Regarding the disjoint union of cyclic graphs, the behaviour of the dominant contributions depends on the size of the bipartitions associated with the graphs appearing in the union. In particular, if there are at least two cyclic graphs associated with a balanced bipartition ( i.e. a bipartition of the form H “ HB b HB with |B| “ D{2 and D even), then the disjoint union does not have exclusively tree-like dominant graphs. Otherwise, the dominant graphs are tree-like. This result arises because cyclic graphs associated with a balanced bipartition behave like square matrix models, whereas cyclic graphs associated with an unbalanced bipartition behave like rectangular matrix models (see Ref. [64, 119]). Before proceeding, we highlight an important result concerning tree-like dominant families of colored graphs. If 

G1 has (only) tree-like dominant graphs and G2 is an arbitrary D-colored graph, then the disjoint union G1 \ G2

also has (only) tree-like dominant graphs. As an illustration, consider G1 “ RM p1,2q

Among the invariants of Sec. 3, it is known that melonic invariants, moments of the partial transpose and joint realignment moments are all compatible. 82 6.3 Applications 

6.3.1 Large N approximation of LO -monotones in the Haar-random state 

In this subsection, we fix D ě 2 and let φ def 

“ p| φN yq N PN˚ denote the following sequence of Haar-distributed random states: for any N P N˚, |φN y is Haar-distributed on a D-partite state space of local dimension N .In Sec. 2.7, we introduced the family of LO -monotones tRGuGPGD , which can be understood as a multipartite generalization of the family of bipartite entanglement R´ enyi entropies (see Exs. 2.34 and 2.35). Given G P GD , we will be interested in the following related questions: 1. What is the typical value of RGp| φN yq in the asymptotic regime of large local dimension N Ñ 8 ?2. Under which condition on G the asymptotic equivalence relation 

xRGp| φN yqy „  

> NÑ8

´ ln px Tr Gp| φN yqyq (353) may hold? In the bipartite setting, it is well known that the typical value of the entanglement R´ enyi-k entropy (with k ě 2 an integer), 

Spkq 

> R

p| φN yq “ 1

k ´ 1 RCk p| φN yq , (354) is ´ 1 

> k´1

ln px Tr Ck p| φN yqyq “ ln pN q ´ 1 

> k´1

ln pCat kq ` O p1{N q (see Ref. [29]). Moreover, Eq. (353) does hold with 

G “ Ck. By contrast, we will see that answering the previous two questions is more difficult when D ě 3: arguments based on concentration phenomena that allow us to answer the first question does not apply to any graph G; furthermore, the validity of Eq. (353) is not directly implied by a good control over the first question. While our main focus will be on the sequence φ of Haar-distributed states introduced at the beginning of this section, we will prove general results that may be applied to other sequences of random states. In particular, random tensor networks [30] (see also e.g. Refs. [36, 38, 120]), which are defined in terms of collections of independently and identically distributed Haar (or Gaussian) random tensors, fall in the category of models that are computable enough for the key large-N factorization assumption made in Eq. (355) to be rigorously established (for simple enough families of graphs). Furthermore, the Haar-distributed random sequence φ can be understood as the simplest possible random tensor network, comprising one bulk node and D boundary nodes. Factorization results have already been investigated in some detail for specific invariants in this context ( e.g. cyclic graphs for general random tensor networks in Ref. [30], or multi-entropy invariants for Haar-distributed states in Ref. [94]), but it would be valuable to explore this question more systematically in the future. The next definition introduces the main restriction we will impose on the choices of distribution and trace-invariant. 

Definition 6.6. Let ψ “ p| ψN yq N PN˚ denote a sequence of random D-partite states and G P GD . We will say that 

pG, ψ q obeys the large-N factorization criterion whenever: 65 

xTr Gp| ψN yq Tr Gp| ψN yqy “  

> NÑ8

xTr Gp| ψN yqy x Tr Gp| ψN yqy p 1 ` O p1{N qq . (355) 

Remark 6.7. One trivially has that: pG, ψ q obeys the large-N factorization criterion if and only if pG, ψ q does. 

Assuming a large-N Ansatz, the next elementary lemma expresses the large-N factorization criterion in terms of the coefficients appearing in the large-N Ansatz.

Typical value of RG in the Haar-random state. Let us start out by quoting the following concentration phenomenon result. 

Proposition 6.11. Let ψ “ p| ψN yq N PN˚ be a sequence of random D-partite states and G P GD . If pG, ψ q obeys both the large-N Ansatz (51) and the large-N factorization criterion (355) (or equivalently (357) ), then: for any

|sGpψq| ln pN q ´ ln |μGpψq| . (361) 

Proof. The proof takes its inspiration from Ref. [30, p. 33], in that its key ingredient is Markov’s inequality, applied to the random variable |Tr Gp| ψN yq ||μGpψq|N sGpψq ´ 1 . The interested reader is referred to the companion paper [46]. Specializing to the Haar-distributed state, we obtain the following immediate Corollary. 

Corollary 6.12. Let G P GD be such that pG, φ q obeys the large-N factorization criterion (Eq. (358) ). Then, the typical value of RGp| φN yq in the large-N regime is 

|sGpφq| ln pN q ´ ln |μGpφq| . (362) In the bipartite case, Eq. (358) holds for any colored graph G P G2. However, as recently proven in Ref. [31], in the multipartite context there exist trace-invariants for which Eq. (358) does not hold. 66 This has two interesting consequences. First, there does not exist any sequence of deterministic quantum states ψ “ p| ψN yq N PN˚ that is LU -equivalent in scaling (resp. asymptotically LU -equivalent) to the sequence of Haar-random states φ “ p| φN yq N PN˚ ,as was already pointed out in Sec. 3.4. Second, using concentration of measure phenomena to approximate averages of non-linear functions of trace-invariants is a viable strategy for those invariants for which Eq. (358) is known to hold. In the companion paper Ref. [46], we provide a number of sufficient conditions for such large-N factorization criterion to hold.   

> 66 In fact, Ref. [31] relies on a probabilistic proof, that establishes a much stronger result: in informal terms, a large graph Gchosen uniformly at random will fail to obey the conditions of Eq. (357) with probabilty close to 1 (we refer the interested reader to Ref. [31] for a precise statement).

6.3.2 LU -inequivalent reference states 

In the search for characterization of LU -equivalence classes of reference states, we relied in Thm. 3.3 on the colored graphs associated with the multi-entropy and reflected multi-entropy families. Our goal here is to clarify if and how the tree construction of trace-invariants can be used for the same purpose. The guiding idea in Thm. 3.3 was to characterize the LU -equivalence between |ψαy and |ψβ y by analyzing the sum ΣpGq def

Remark 6.22. These D equations coincide with those produced by the melonic family: the tree construction with flips automatically encodes the melonic constraints, so that any non-melonic generator is sufficient to generate them. Consequently, flips allow one to reduce the number of independent trace-invariants that must be specified, although the total number of independent equations required remains 2D ´ D ´ 1.

87 In the proof of Thm. 3.3, a naive choice involves, for D “ 3, five invariants ME 32, ME 33, and the three RE pcq

> 2,1

yielding one redundant equation. More economical choices are possible, for instance by discarding one reflected R´ enyi invariant, or by using PT 3 together with the three melonic invariants at k “ 2, both leading to four equations for four unknowns. The flip-based tree construction shows that even this can be simplified further: a single non-melonic invariant, such as PT 3, is sufficient. Indeed, flips automatically generate the graphs PT 3 , PT 3 ˚1 PT 3 , PT 3 ˚2 PT 3 , and PT 3 ˚3 PT 3 , (382) which yield four independent equations, including the three melonic constraints. Thus, while flips do not reduce the total number of required equations, they minimize the number of independent trace-invariants that must be chosen.

> 69

We note that, though convenient, the full power of Weingarten’s calculus is not necessary to establish the previous result. Amore elementary proof can be obtained as follows: 1) the operator ş dU pU |ϕ0y x ϕ0| U :qbk commutes with the diagonal action of the unitary group on Hbk , and the same is true on the symmetric subspace of Hbk (i.e. the subspace of states that are invariant under permutations of the tensor factors); 2) the symmetric subspace is irreducible in that representation, so by Schur’s lemma, one may infer that ş dU pU |ϕ0y x ϕ0| U :qbk is proportional to the orthogonal projector on the symmetric subspace; 3) the trace of the previous equation can be easily integrated, which fixes the multiplicative factor; 4) plugging this result into Eq. (399) directly yields Eq. (398), bypassing the introduction of the Weingarten function. See e.g. Ref. [123] for further detail.

(405) The normalization implies that x∥X∥y2 “ 1, but this normalization holds only on average, unlike the Haar-random state |φy for which ∥|φy∥ “ 1 with probability 1, so that |Xy cannot be considered a random quantum state. The two tensor distributions agree at first order at large N though, in the sense that Eq. (405) and Eq. (398) differ only by a factor fk,D,N , which for k, D fixed goes to 1 when N goes to infinity, so that the moments characterizing the distributions differ by the same factor:

# B On the p-complete degree 

Let G P GD be a D-colored graph with kpGq white vertices. This section is focused on a generalized version of the Gurau degree called p-complete degree and denoted by ωppGq, 2 ď p ď D ´ 1, introduced in Sec. 4.1, Eq. (214) as: 

ωppGq “

κpG|c1,...,c p q , (408) Due to their trivial definitions, we do not include in this appendix the cases p “ 1 and p “ D introduced in Sec. 4. From the definition, it is clear that ωppGq P Z for any D-colored graph. Melonic and planar graphs have been introduced in Sec. 3.1. Inspired by the proofs of Ref. [105], this appendix aims to prove the following theorem: 

Theorem B.1. For all D-colored graph G and 2 ď p ď D ´ 1, the p-complete degree is non-negative and vanishes if and only if G is melonic ( D ą 3) or planar ( D “ 3).

(413) This is indeed true: for i1 ă ¨ ¨ ¨ ă ip P t c1, . . . , c q u, the term κpp G|c1,...,c q q| i1,...,i p q appears as many times as there are ways to complete i1, . . . , i p to q colors, that is, to choose q ´ p colors among the remaining D ´ p.A property of ωp that will be needed is its invariance under 1 -dipole contractions (this is known for ω2, see 

e.g. Ref. [105]). 

Definition B.3. If G P GD and c P t 1, . . . , D u, Gˆc denotes the graph without the edges of color c.– A 1-dipole is a single edge e “ p v, v q such that v and v belong to different connected components of Gˆc.– An edge contraction is the operation that removes an edge e “ p v, v q and the vertices v and v, and connects the pending edges respecting the coloring (Fig. 48). The inverse operation is an edge insertion. – A 1-dipole contraction (insertion) is the contraction (insertion) of a 1-dipole. 

Figure 48: Edge insertion and contraction. 

Lemma B.4. For 2 ď p ď D ´ 1, the p-complete degree is invariant under 1-dipole contractions and insertions. Proof. Let G P GD and rG resulting from the contraction of a 1-dipole e of color c in G. One has kpGq ´ kp rGq “ 1.

Furthermore, since e is a 1-dipole, v and v belong to two different color i, j faces of G for any two i, j different from 

c, so that in rG the edges of color i, j belong to the same color i, j face. The operation therefore, leaves the number of connected components unchanged: 

κpGq “ κp rGq . (414) The same is true for any subgraph G|C with C Ă t 1, . . . , D u, c P C: κpG|C q “ κp rG|C q. If, however, c R C, then the edge-contraction reduces the number of connected components by one: κpG|C q “ κp rG|C q ` 1. As a consequence: 

κppqpGq ´ κppqp rGq “ 

ˆD ´ 1

Lemma B.5. For any D-colored graph G, we have ωD´1pGq P N. Moreover, for D ě 3, we have the equivalence 

ωD´1pGq “ 0 ðñ ω2pGq “ 0 . (417) 93 Proof. We first prove that ωD´1pGq ě 0 (it is an integer by definition). Starting from a graph G, we choose a 1-dipole and contract it, and do so inductively until there are no more 1-dipoles, and we call H the resulting graph. At each step, both kpGq ´ kpHq and κpD´1qpGq ´ κpD´1qpHq grow by one (Eq. (415)), so that 

kpGq ´ kpHq “ κpD´1qpGq ´ κpD´1qpHq . (418) However, since there is no 1-dipole in H, removing the edges of any color leaves the number of connected components unchanged, that is, for any c, κpH ˆcq “ κpHq “ κpGq, where the second equality follows from Eq. (414). As a consequence: 

κpD´1qpHq “ Dκ pGq . (419) Combining this with Eq. (418), we may express ωD´1 given in Eq. (416) as

remains the same under such operations (Lem. B.4 and Ref. [105]), we conclude that ω2pGq “ 0.

# D Proof of Thm. 6.4 

This section is subdivided in two parts. First, we prove a result regarding the disjoint union and the flip operation for graphs having tree-like dominant graphs and regarding the vertex contraction operation for graphs having only 

tree-like dominant graphs. Secondly, we prove the statement of Thm. 6.4 for compatible graphs or graphs with bounds on the degree of compatibility.

## D.2 Results on graphs with bounded degrees of compatibility 

For the union operation and the flip operation, a generic result can be stated as long as the degree of compatibility of A and B is upper bounded. 

Proposition D.5. Let D ě 3, A, B P GD and peA, e B q be vertices of A and B.1. If either ∆pAq ` ∆pBq ă DpD ´ 1q{ 2 or ∆pA \ Bq ă DpD ´ 1q{ 2 then: 

∆pA \ Bq “ ∆pAq ` ∆pBq and MpA \ Bq “ MpAq \ MpBq . (453)

∆pA ˚peA,e B q Bq “ ∆pAq ` ∆pBq and MpA ˚peA,e B q Bq “ MpAq ˚ peA,e B q MpBq . (454)
